Rulings on Kinayah Words and Describing Divorce
Lists rulings on kinayah, intention, intensity, and attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on kinayah words of divorce, the role of intention, the effect of describing intensity, and the rule when divorce is added to a whole or a common part.

Core Idea
Kinayah words require intention or context; describing divorce as intense makes it irrevocable; divorcing a part counts as full divorce.

In this lesson, we examine a key passage from Al-Quduri's matn on divorce. The text defines which words are kinayah (allusive) for divorce, when intention is required, and the legal consequences of describing divorce with intensity or attributing it to a part of the wife. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correctly applying the Hanafi school's rulings on ṭalāq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of kinayah words
Read the passage and note the words listed as kinayah: bā'in, batata, batlah, harām, etc. Understand that these are not explicit words for divorce.
The list is not exhaustive; 'etc.' indicates other similar words.
2
Distinguish the two contexts for intention
The passage states that if the husband utters a kinayah word without intention and they are not in a discussion of divorce, no divorce occurs. If they are in a discussion, divorce occurs in court but not before Allah unless intended. If they are in anger or dispute, divorce occurs unless the word was meant as insult.
The court ruling is based on apparent circumstances; divine accountability depends on actual intention.
3
Analyze the effect of describing intensity
When the husband describes the divorce with words of intensity (e.g., 'most intense divorce', 'Satan's divorce'), the divorce becomes bā'in (irrevocable).
This is a Hanafi ruling; describing intensity makes the divorce irrevocable even if it is the first divorce.
4
Apply the rule for adding divorce to a whole or part
If the husband attributes divorce to the wife's whole (e.g., 'your neck is divorced') or to a common part (e.g., 'half of you is divorced'), divorce occurs fully. Divorce is indivisible.
This reinforces that ṭalāq is not divisible; a fractional divorce counts as one complete divorce.
5
Connect to evidence from the margins
The passage notes that proofs from hadith and Companions are in the margins. For a rigorous study, consult those proofs, e.g., the hadith of Rifā'ah and the incident with 'Umar regarding 'habluki 'alā ghāribik'.
This step is for deeper verification; the matn itself is authoritative from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الطَّلَاقِ
وَهَذَا مِثْلُ قَوْلِهِ أَنْتِ بَائِنٌ وَبَتَةٌ وَبَتْلَةٌ وَحَرَامٌ وَحَبْلُكِ عَلَى غَارِبِكِ وَالْقِي بِأَهْلِكَ وَخَلِيَّةٌ وَبَرِيَّةٌ إِلَى آخِرِهِ
فَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ لَهُ نِيَّةٌ لَمْ يَقَعْ بِهَذِهِ الْأَلْفَاظِ طَلَاقٌ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَا فِي مُذَاكَرَةِ الطَّلَاقِ فَيَقَعُ بِمَا الطلاق في الْقَضَاءِ وَلَا يَقَعُ فِيمَا بَيْنَهُ وَبَيْنَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى إِلَّا أَنْ يَنْوِيَهُ
وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُونَا فِي مُذَاكَرَةِ الطَّلَاقِ وَكَانَا فِي غَصْبٍ أَوْ خُصُومَةٍ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ بِكُلِّ لَفْظَةٍ لَا يَقْصِدُ بِمَا السَّبَّ وَالشَّتِيمَةَ
وَإِذَا وَصَفَ الطَّلَاقَ بِضَرْبٍ مِنْ الزِّيَادَةِ وَالشَّدَّةِ كَانَ بَائِنًا مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ بَائِنْ أَوْ طَالِقٌ أَشَدَّ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ أَفْحَشَ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ طَلَاقَ الشَّيْطَانِ أَوْ طَلَاقَ الْبِدْعَةِ أَوْ كَاخُبْل أَوْ مِلْءِ الْبَيْتِ
وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى جُمْلَتِهَا أَوْ إِلَى مَا يُعَبَّرُ بِهِ عَنْ الْجُمْلَةِ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ رَقَبَتُكِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ عُنُقُكِ أَوْ رُوحُكِ أَوْ جَسَدُكِ أَوْ فَرْجُكِ أَوْ وَجْهِكِ
وَوَكَذَا إِنْ طَلَّقَ جُزْءًا شَائِعًا مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ نِصْفُكِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ تُلْتُكِ

وقد وردت أدلة كثيرة من الحديث وأقوال الصحابة في هوامش الصفحات.
Book of Divorce. And this is like his saying: 'You are bā'in,' and 'batata,' and 'batlah,' and 'harām,' and 'your rope is on your shoulders,' and 'join your family,' and 'khaliyyah,' and 'bariyyah,' etc. If he has no intention, divorce does not occur with these words unless they are in a discussion of divorce, then it occurs in court but not between him and Allah unless he intends it. And if they are not in a discussion of divorce and are in anger or a dispute, divorce occurs with every word that does not intend insult or abuse. And if he describes divorce with a type of increase and intensity, it becomes irrevocable (bā'in), like if he says: 'You are divorced – irrevocably,' or 'divorced most intensely,' or 'most obscene divorce,' or 'divorce of Satan,' or 'innovative divorce,' or 'like khaḍl' or 'fill of the house.' And if he adds divorce to her whole or to what expresses the whole, divorce occurs, like if he says: 'You are divorced,' or 'your neck is divorced,' or 'your neck,' or 'your soul,' or 'your body,' or 'your private part,' or 'your face.' And similarly if he divorces a common part, like if he says: 'Half of you is divorced,' or 'a third of you.' And many proofs from hadith and sayings of the Companions are noted in the margins.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage from al-Quduri's matn on divorce covers several key rulings. First, it lists words that are kinayah (allusive) for divorce: bā'in, batata, batlah, harām, etc. These words do not effect divorce without intention (niyyah) unless the couple is in a discussion of divorce (mudhākarat al-ṭalāq), in which case a judge would rule divorce occurs, but before Allah it only counts if the husband intended it. If they are not discussing divorce but are in a context of anger or dispute (ghaḍab or khuṣūmah), then every word that is not meant as insult or abuse causes divorce. Second, if the husband describes divorce with intensity (e.g., 'most intense divorce,' 'Satan's divorce'), the divorce becomes bā'in (irrevocable). Third, if he attributes divorce to her whole being (e.g., 'your neck is divorced') or to a common fractional part (e.g., 'half of you is divorced'), divorce takes effect fully. The Hanafi position is that divorce is not divisible; even saying 'half a divorce' counts as one complete divorce. The marginal notes provide hadith and athar evidence, such as the hadith of Rifā'ah and the incident of Hubal 'alā ghāribik with 'Umar. The relied-upon position (muftā bihī) in the Hanafi school is that these rulings are from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-ṭalāq): The dissolution of marriage by the husband's utterance.
كنايات الطلاق (kināyāt al-ṭalāq): Allusive words that imply divorce but require intention to effect it.
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back his wife without a new marriage.
نيۃ (niyyah): Intention in the heart; required for kinayah words to effect divorce except in specific contexts.
جزء شائع (juz' shā'i'): An undivided fraction of a whole, e.g., half or third.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'أنت طالق أشد الطلاق' (you are divorced most intensely). According to the Hanafi school, this divorce is:
A) Revocable (raj'ī)
B) Irrevocable (bā'in)
C) Invalid because of exaggeration
D) Only effective if he intends three divorces

Key Takeaway
Kinayah words require intention or context; describing divorce with intensity makes it irrevocable; divorcing a part of the wife counts as a full divorce.

Talaq — Divorce

Rulings on Describing Divorce with Hyperbole and Adding to Totality
States that various hyperbolic and part-referring expressions effect divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on hyperbolic divorce expressions and explain why attributing divorce to a part of the wife effects divorce on the whole.

Core Idea
Any expression that clearly indicates divorce, even hyperbolic or referring to a part, effects divorce on the whole woman.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn lists various unusual divorce phrases that are nonetheless valid in Hanafi law. It also clarifies that when a husband mentions a part of his wife (e.g., her neck or half of her) the divorce falls on her entire person. Understanding these rulings helps you recognize the broad scope of divorce expressions that carry legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hyperbolic expressions listed
Read the opening lines: 'ashadd al-talaq', 'afhash al-talaq', 'talaq al-shaytan', etc. Note that all are considered valid expressions of divorce.
These phrases indicate exaggeration but do not negate the intent to divorce.
2
Understand the principle of totality and common part
The passage states that if divorce is attributed to the wife's totality (e.g., 'raqabatik', 'ruhik', 'jasadik') or to a common part (e.g., 'nisfuk', 'thulthuk'), divorce still occurs. This shows that referring to a part refers to the whole.
The key principle: divorce is not divisible; it falls on the entire person.
3
Recognize the evidenciary support
The passage cites Quranic verses using 'raqaba' (neck) and 'wajh' (face) to refer to the whole person, and hadith/athar from Companions and Tabi'in confirming that such expressions effect divorce.
These evidences justify the Hanafi position that any clear reference to the wife, even a part, effects divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ طَالِقٌ أَشَدَّ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ أَفْحَشَ الطَّلَاقِ أَوْ طَلَاقَ الشَّيْطَانِ أَوْ طَلَاقَ الْبِدْعَةِ أَوْ كَاخُبْل أَوْ مِلْءِ الْبَيْتِ وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى جُمْلَتِهَا أَوْ إِلَى مَا يُعَبَّرُ بِهِ عَنْ الْجُمْلَةِ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ رَقَبَتُكِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ عُنُقُكِ أَوْ رُوحُكِ أَوْ جَسَدُكِ أَوْ فَرْجُكِ أَوْ وَجْهِكِ وَكَذَا إِنْ طَلَّقَ جُزْءًا شَائِعًا مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ نِصْفُكِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ تُلْتُكِ 
وَجْهٌ (١) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَإِذَا وَصَفَ الطَّلَاقَ بِضَرْبٍ مِنَ الزِّيَادَةِ: عَنْ عَائِشَةَ، فِي رَجُلٍ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ وَاحِدَةً كَأَلْفٍ، قَالَ: «لَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ حَتَّى تَنْكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ شَيْبَةَ: مَا قَالُوا: فِي الرَّجُلِ يَقُولُ لِامْرَأَتِهِ: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَاحِدَةً كَأَلْفٍ وَطَالِقٌ حِمْلَ بَعِيرٍ، رَقَم ١٨٢٢٢)
وَجْهٌ (٢) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ حِمْلِ بَعِيرٍ: عَنْ عَلِيٍّ فِي رَجُلٍ طَلَّقَ امْرَأَتَهُ حِمْلَ بَعِيرٍ، قَالَ: «لَا تَحِلُّ لَهُ حَتَّى تَنْكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ شَيْبَةَ: مَا قَالُوا فِي الرَّجُلِ يَقُولُ لِامْرَأَتِهِ: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَاحِدَةً كَأَلْفٍ وَطَالِقٌ حِمْلَ بَعِيرٍ، رَقَم ١٨٢٢١)
وَجْهٌ (٣) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ: ﴿وَمَنْ قَتَلَ مُؤْمِنًا خَطَأً فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ مُؤْمِنَةٍ وَدِيَةٌ مُسَلَّمَةٌ إِلَى أَهْلِهِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَصَّدَّقُوا﴾ (النساء ٩٢)
وَجْهٌ (٤) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ: ﴿فَظَلَّتْ أَعْنَاقُهُمْ لَهَا خَاضِعِينَ﴾ (الشعراء ٤)
وَجْهٌ (٥) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ: ﴿وَعَنَتِ الْوُجُوهُ لِلْحَيِّ الْقَيُّومِ﴾ (طه ١١١)
وَجْهٌ (٦) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ: عَنْ قَتَادَةَ قَالَ: «إِذَا قَالَ: إِصْبَعُكِ طَالِقٌ فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ، قَدْ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ عَلَيْهَا» (مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ: بَابُ يُطَلِّقُ بَعْضَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ، رَقَم ١١٢٥٢)
وَجْهٌ (٧) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَكَذَا إِنْ طَلَّقَ جُزْءًا شَائِعًا: عَنِ الشَّعْبِيِّ، قَالَ: «إِذَا قَالَ: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ نِصْفًا، أَوْ ثُلُثَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ فَهِيَ تَطْلِيقَةٌ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ شَيْبَةَ: رَقَم ١٨٠٦١، مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ: رَقَم ١١٢٥١)
أُصُولٌ: طَلَاقٌ مُتَجَزِّئٌ لَا يَكُونُ، يَعْنِي آدَھَا یَا تَھَائِي یَا چَوْتھَائِي حِصَّہ پَر نَہِیں ہوتی بَلْکَہ پُورے جِسْم پَر واقِع ہوتی ہَے۔
Book of Divorce: Or 'divorced with the severest divorce', or 'most obscene divorce', or 'divorce of Satan', or 'innovative divorce', or 'like the umbilical cord?', or 'fill of the house'. And if he attributes divorce to her totality or to what expresses totality, divorce occurs, such as saying: 'You are divorced', or 'Your neck is divorced', or 'your neck', or 'your spirit', or 'your body', or 'your private part', or 'your face'. Similarly, if he divorces a common part, such as saying: 'Half of you is divorced' or 'a third of you is divorced'. Evidence 1: The statement of a Companion for establishing 'and if he describes divorce with a type of addition' - from 'A'isha regarding a man who divorced his wife one divorce like a thousand; he said: 'She is not lawful for him until she marries another husband.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba: number 18222). Evidence 2: The statement of a Companion for establishing 'load of a camel' - from 'Ali regarding a man who divorced his wife with 'load of a camel'; he said: 'She is not lawful for him until she marries another husband.' (number 18221). Evidence 3: The verse for establishing 'such as his saying: you are divorced': 'And whoever kills a believer by mistake, then the freeing of a believing slave and compensation delivered to his family unless they give charity' (an-Nisa 4:92). Evidence 4: The verse: 'So their necks became humbled to it' (ash-Shu'ara 26:4). Evidence 5: The verse: 'And faces will be humbled before the Living, the Self-Sustaining' (Ta-Ha 20:111). Evidence 6: The statement of a Tabi'i (Qatada): 'If he says: your finger is divorced, she is divorced; divorce has occurred upon her.' (Musannaf 'Abd ar-Razzaq: number 11252). Evidence 7: The statement of a Tabi'i (ash-Sha'bi): 'If he says: you are divorced by half or one-third of a divorce, then it is one divorce.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba: 18061, Musannaf 'Abd ar-Razzaq: 11251). Principle: Divorce is not divisible; meaning it does not occur on half, third, or quarter portion but rather occurs on the entire body.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Quduri stating the Hanafi position on expressions of divorce that include hyperbolic or descriptive phrases, as well as divorcing the wife by reference to her totality or a common part of her. The ruling is that all such expressions effect a valid divorce. The text then provides scriptural and traditional evidence. The passage opens by listing various hyperbolic or unusual phrases: 'the severest divorce', 'most obscene divorce', 'divorce of Satan', 'innovative divorce', 'like the umbilical cord?', 'fill of the house'. All of these are considered valid expressions of divorce and trigger its occurrence. Then it states that if the husband attributes divorce to her totality or to what expresses totality—such as saying 'your neck is divorced', 'your spirit', 'your body', 'your private part', 'your face'—divorce occurs. Similarly, if he divorces a common part from her, like 'half of you is divorced' or 'a third of you', divorce also occurs. The commentary then provides the evidenciary support: hadith from 'A'isha and 'Ali about analogous expressions, Quranic verses using the word 'raqaba' (neck) and 'wajh' (face) to show that these terms refer to the whole person, and statements of the tabi'in Qatada and ash-Sha'bi affirming that specifying a part does not prevent divorce from taking effect on the whole. The final note in Urdu clarifies the principle that divorce is not divisible; it does not fall on a fractional share of the woman but on her entire person.

Key Terms
جُمْلَة (jumla): The totality of the wife's person, as opposed to a part.
جُزْء شَائِع (juz' shā'i'): A common part, such as half or third, meaning an undivided fraction of the wife.
طَلَاق الشَّيْطَان (talāq al-shayṭān): A divorce that is conditional or sworn in a manner mimicking Satanic deception, e.g., 'you are divorced if I do X'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Your neck is divorced' (رقبتك طالق), what is the ruling in the Hanafi school?
A) Divorce does not occur because 'neck' is not the whole person.
B) Divorce occurs because 'neck' expresses her totality.
C) Divorce occurs only if he also intends divorce.
D) Divorce does not occur unless he says 'you are divorced'.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, any expression that clearly conveys the intention to divorce, whether hyperbolic or by referring to a part, effects divorce on the wife's entire person.

Talaq and Its Indivisibility: Limb Divorce and Fractional Talaq
States that divorcing a non-totality part does not effect divorce, but divorcing half a talaq counts as one full talaq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorcing a specific body part and on fractional talaq, and apply the principle that talaq is indivisible.

Core Idea
Talaq is indivisible; a fractional talaq counts as one full talaq, and a limb's divorce is void.

This passage from al-Quduri's matn presents two succinct rulings on the nature of talaq. The first clarifies that talaq only applies to the whole person, not to body parts. The second establishes that talaq cannot be fractionally assigned – any fraction is rounded up to one full talaq. Understanding these rulings reveals a core Hanafi principle: talaq is an indivisible legal act.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two distinct cases: (a) divorcing a limb (hand, foot) and (b) divorcing by a fraction (half, third).
2
Extract the rulings
For the limb scenario, the ruling is that no divorce occurs. For the fractional scenario, the ruling is that she becomes divorced by one full talaq.
3
Understand the rationale
The underlying principle is that talaq is a holistic revocation of the marriage. It cannot be localized to a part of the wife, nor can it be divided into fractions. Therefore, any attempt to do so either fails entirely or is treated as a complete talaq.
4
Memorize the evidence
Recall that the passage supports the fractional ruling with the practice of the tabi'un: Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz and ash-Sha'bi both ruled that half a talaq is one full talaq.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ يَدُكِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ رِجْلُكِ طَالِقٌ لَا يَقَعُ الطَّلَاقُ وَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا نِصْفَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ أَوْ ثُلُثَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ كَانَتْ طَالِقًا وَاحِدًا
وَجْهٌ (١) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ وَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا نِصْفَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ: قِيلَ لِعُمَرَ بْنِ عَبْدِ الْعَزِيزِ: الرَّجُلُ يُطَلِّقُ امْرَأَتَهُ نِصْفَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ، قَالَ: «هِيَ تَطْلِيقَةٌ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ: رَقَم ١٨٠٥٩، مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ: رَقَم ١١٢٥١)
وَجْهٌ (٢) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِ مِثْلِهِ: عَنِ الشَّعْبِيِّ، قَالَ: «إِذَا قَالَ: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ نِصْفًا، أَوْ ثُلُثَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ فَهِيَ تَطْلِيقَةٌ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ: رَقَم ١٨٠٦١)
And if he says: 'Your hand is divorced' or 'your foot is divorced', divorce does not occur. And if he divorces her by half a divorce or a third of a divorce, she becomes divorced by one divorce. Evidence 1: The statement of a Tabi'i (Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz) regarding a man who divorces his wife half a divorce: he said: 'It is one divorce.' Evidence 2: The statement of a Tabi'i (ash-Sha'bi): 'If he says: you are divorced half or one-third of a divorce, then it is one divorce.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings from the matn of al-Quduri. First, if a husband says 'your hand is divorced' or 'your foot is divorced', no divorce occurs because these are specific limbs that do not represent the totality of the wife. Second, if he says 'you are divorced by half a talaq' or 'a third of a talaq', it counts as one full talaq, not a fraction. The commentary provides support from the practice of the caliph Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz and the tabi'i ash-Sha'bi, both of whom ruled that a fractional talaq is treated as a complete talaq. The underlying principle is that talaq is not divisible; it either occurs fully or not at all. Thus, fractions of a talaq are rounded up to one full talaq. This distinguishes talaq from other legal actions that may be partial, such as manumission.

Key Terms
تَطْلِيقَة (taṭlīqa): A single pronouncement of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife 'Your hand is divorced' (يدك طالق), what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs because the hand is part of her.
B) Divorce does not occur because the hand is not totality.
C) Divorce occurs only if he intends divorce.
D) Divorce does not occur unless the divorce is by talaq.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, talaq cannot be assigned to a part of the wife or fractioned; any fractional pronouncement counts as one full talaq.

Coercion and Intoxication Do Not Nullify Divorce
Ruling with evidence and inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced or intoxicated divorce and explain why the school distinguishes between sin and legal effect.

Core Idea
Coercion or intoxication does not negate the legal effect of divorce words.

A man pronounces divorce under duress — does it count? The Hanafi school says yes, because the words themselves trigger the legal consequence. This lesson unpacks the ruling, the evidence from early scholars, and the key dispute with the Shafi'i school. You will learn why the school insists on the effectiveness of such pronouncements despite the lack of free will.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
State the ruling from the matn: the divorce of a coerced or intoxicated person is effective (waqi'). Identify the act (talaq) and the category (sahih).
2
Examine the Hanafi Evidence
Review the cited statements from Ibrahim an-Nakha'i and Umar ibn al-Khattab. Understand that these show the early understanding that coercion does not remove legal liability for divorce.
These are qawl sahabi and qawl tabi'i, which are considered proofs in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the Opposing Proof
Read the Shafi'i evidence: Quran 16:106 and the hadith of forgiveness under coercion. Note that these argue for nullity of coerced acts.
4
Understand the Hanafi Response
Grasp the distinction: the Quran and hadith address sin and faith, not legal rulings. Divorce is a legal act that takes effect once uttered, regardless of intention or coercion.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in Hanafi, one divorce counts; in Shafi'i, it does not.
5
Apply the Principle
Conclude that in Hanafi law, a coerced or intoxicated divorce reduces the triple count. Recognize the practical consequence for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ: وَطَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ وَالسَّكْرَانِ وَاقِعٌ
وَجْهٌ (١) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ لِثُبُوتِهِ: عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، قَالَ: «طَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ جَائِزٌ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ: رَقَم ١٨٠٤٠، مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ: رَقَم ١١٤١٩)
وَجْهٌ (٢) قَوْلُ التَّابِعِيِّ: عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ قَالَ: «هُوَ جَائِزٌ، إِنَّمَا هُوَ شَيْءٌ افْتَدَى بِهِ نَفْسَهُ» (مُصَنَّفُ ابْنِ أَبِي شَيْبَةَ: رَقَم ١٨٠٤١)
وَجْهٌ (٣) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ وَالتَّابِعِيِّ: عَنْ شُرَيْحٍ قَالَ: «الْقَيْدُ كُرْهٌ، وَالْوَعِيدُ كُرْهٌ، وَالسِّجْنُ كُرْهٌ» قَالَ عُمَرُ بْنُ الْخَطَّابِ: «لَيْسَ الرَّجُلُ أَمِينًا عَلَى نَفْسِهِ إِذَا أَجَعْتَهُ، أَوْ أَوْثَقْتَهُ، أَوْ ضَرَبْتَهُ» (مُصَنَّفُ عَبْدِ الرَّزَّاقِ: رَقَم ١١٤٢٣-١١٤٢٤)
وَجْهٌ (٤) دَلِيلُ الشَّافِعِيِّ: الْآيَةُ: ﴿إِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَئِنٌّ بِالْإِيمَانِ﴾ (النحل ١٠٦) وَالْحَدِيثُ: عَنْ أَبِي ذَرٍّ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إِنَّ اللَّهَ قَدْ تَجَاوَزَ عَنْ أُمَّتِي الْخَطَأَ، وَالنِّسْيَانَ، وَمَا اسْتُكْرِهُوا عَلَيْهِ» (سُنَنُ ابْنِ مَاجَهْ: رَقَم ٢٠٤٣)
وَجْهٌ (٥) قَوْلُ الصَّحَابِيِّ: وَقَالَ ابْنُ عَبَّاسٍ: «طَلَاقُ السَّكْرَانِ وَالْمُسْتَكْرَهِ لَيْسَ بِجَائِزٍ» (صَحِيحُ الْبُخَارِيِّ: رَقَم ٥٢٦٩)
أُصُولٌ: طَلَاقٌ مُجْبَرًا عَلَيْهِ یَا نَشَہ مَیں مُسْتَ ہو كَر طَلَاق دِیَا تو طَلَاق واقِع ہو جائے گي، بَرْخِلَافِ إِمَامِ شَافِعِی کے۔
Section: And the divorce of the coerced and the intoxicated is effective. Evidence 1: Statement of the Tabi'i Ibrahim: 'The divorce of the coerced is permissible.' Evidence 2: Ibrahim said: 'It is permissible; indeed it is something by which he ransomed himself.' Evidence 3: Statement of the Companion and Tabi'i: Shuraih said: 'Fetters are coercion, threats are coercion, prison is coercion.' Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'A man is not trustworthy over himself if you starve him, tie him up, or beat him.' Evidence 4: The proof of al-Shafi'i: The verse 'Except one who is coerced while his heart is firm in faith' (an-Nahl 16:106) and the hadith from Abu Dharr: 'Indeed Allah has forgiven my community for mistakes, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into.' Evidence 5: Statement of the Companion: Ibn 'Abbas said: 'The divorce of the intoxicated and the coerced is not permissible.' Principle: If divorce is compelled or given while intoxicated, divorce will occur, contrary to Imam al-Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the matn of al-Quduri, followed by a discussion of evidence including inter-madhhab dispute. The ruling states that the divorce of a person who is coerced (mukrah) or intoxicated (sakran) is effective (waqi'). The commentary then provides the Hanafi evidence: statements of the tabi'i Ibrahim an-Nakha'i that such divorce is permissible. It also narrates the position of the Companion Umar ibn al-Khattab and the judge Shuraih that various forms of coercion are recognized. The passage then presents the opposing evidence of Imam al-Shafi'i: the Quranic verse excusing the coerced from disbelief (16:106) and the hadith of Abu Dharr that Allah forgives what is done under coercion. Finally, it cites the Companion Ibn 'Abbas who stated that the divorce of the intoxicated and coerced is not permissible. The passage concludes with a principle (in Urdu) that Hanafi school holds divorce under coercion or intoxication effective, contrary to al-Shafi'i. The Hanafi reasoning is that the hadith about forgiveness applies to sin, not to legal consequences; divorce remains effective because the pronouncement was made. Coercion does not negate the legal effect of the words spoken.

Key Terms
مُكْرَه (mukrah): A person compelled to act under threat of harm to life or limb.
سَكْرَان (sakrān): An intoxicated person who has lost control but still speaks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a man is forced at gunpoint to divorce his wife, what is the ruling on the divorce?
A) It is invalid because he did not intend it.
B) It is valid and counts as a divorce.
C) It is valid only if he says it clearly.
D) It is invalid because coercion negates consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, pronouncing divorce under coercion or intoxication yields a valid divorce; the words spoken carry legal effect irrespective of the speaker's state of free will or consciousness.

Body-Part and Fractional Divorce in Hanafi Fiqh
Two rulings on unusual divorce pronouncements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on divorce pronouncements involving body parts or fractions and apply them to novel scenarios.

Core Idea
Divorce must be explicit and indivisible; attaching it to a body part or splitting it into fractions is legally ineffective.

The Hanafi school strictly regulates the wording of divorce pronouncements. This lesson covers two situations where the pronouncement deviates from the standard: (1) using a body part, and (2) attempting a fractional divorce. The rulings are derived from the principle that divorce is an indivisible legal act that must be directed at the wife herself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pronouncements
Recognize the two types: body-part divorce (e.g., 'your hand is divorced') and fractional divorce (e.g., 'half a divorce').
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For body-part: no divorce occurs. For fractional: it counts as one full divorce.
3
Explain the legal basis
Divorce must be explicitly directed at the wife's person; body-part statements are not direct. Divorce is indivisible by nature, so fractions are rounded to one.
4
Note the supporting evidence
The author cites an athar from 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz who ruled that half a divorce counts as one.
5
Distinguish from other madhahib
Other schools may consider intention in body-part divorce or treat fractional pronouncements as ineffective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ يَدُك طَالِقٌ أَوْ رِجْلُك طَالِقٌ لا يَقَعُ الطَّلَاقُ وَإِنْ طَلَّقَهَا نِصْفَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ أَوْ ثُلُثَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ كَانَتْ طَالِقًا وَاحِدًا قِيلَ لِعُمَرَ بْنِ عَبْدِ الْعَزِيزِ: الرَّجُلُ يُطَلِّقُ امْرَأَتَهُ نِصْفَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ، قَالَ: «هِيَ تَطْلِيقَةٌ»
And if he says 'your hand is divorced' or 'your foot is divorced', divorce does not occur. And if he divorces her half a divorce or third a divorce, it is one divorce. It was said to 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz: A man divorces his wife half a divorce. He said: 'It is a divorce.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the matn of al-Quduri. First, if a man uses body parts in his divorce pronouncement (e.g., 'your hand is divorced'), no divorce occurs because the pronouncement is not legally recognized; divorce must be explicit and direct. Second, if he attempts a fractional divorce (half or third), it counts as one complete divorce, as divorce is an indivisible integer. The author supports the second ruling with an athar from 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-'Aziz, who ruled similarly. These rulings are part of the Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
يَدُك طَالِقٌ (yaduki ṭāliq): A pronouncement attaching divorce to a body part; not a valid talaq in Hanafi fiqh.
نِصْفَ تَطْلِيقَةٍ (niṣfa taṭlīqah): Half a divorce – an attempt to divide the divorce into fractions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Divorce occurs
B) No divorce occurs
C) It depends on his intention
D) It is a revocable divorce

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, divorce must be explicit and whole; any deviation—like attaching it to a body part or splitting it into fractions—results in either no effect or one full divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Divorce of the Coerced and Intoxicated is Effective
Inter-madhhab dispute with proofs and rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced and intoxicated divorce and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-evidence using Quran, Hadith, and athar.

Core Idea
Coercion and intoxication do not invalidate divorce; the utterance alone suffices for legal effect.

The Hanafi school holds that a divorce pronounced under coercion or intoxication is valid (wāqiʿ), contrary to the Shafi'i position. This lesson examines the proofs from the Salaf—Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, Shurayh, and Umar—and the Hanafi response to opposing evidence from the Quran, Hadith of pardon, and athar from Ibn Abbas and Uthman.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the divorce of the coerced (mukrah) and intoxicated (sakrān) is effective. Understand that this is the mufta bihi position.
The text begins with this explicit statement.
2
Examine the Hanafi proofs
Review the athar from Ibrahim, Shurayh, and Umar. Note that Ibrahim considers coerced divorce permissible as a ransom, Shurayh defines coercion (qayd, wa'id, sijn), and Umar states that coercion removes trustworthiness.
These are based on practice of early authorities.
3
Identify the Shafi'i opposition and their evidence
The Shafi'i school argues that coerced divorce is invalid, citing Quran 16:106 (coercion in faith) and the hadith of pardon for the coerced, and athar from Ibn Abbas and Uthman. For intoxication, they analogize with the insane using the hadith of raising the pen.
These are presented in the text as the opposing dalil.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi refutation: the Quranic verse applies to faith and disbelief, not legal acts; the hadith of pardon removes sin but not legal consequences; the intoxicated still utter consciously, unlike the insane or sleeping.
The response is implicit in the text's structure.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: in Hanafi, coerced/intoxicated divorce counts; in Shafi'i, it does not. The Hanafi position is relied upon due to stronger evidence from the Salaf and the principle that utterance suffices.
The text concludes with the principle (uṣūl) supporting the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فَصْلٌ) وَطَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَةِ وَالسَّكْرَانِ وَاقِعٌ. عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ، قَالَ: «طَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ جَائِزٌ» (مصنف ابن شيبه: مَنْ كَانَ يَرَى طَلَاقَ الْمُكْرَهِ جَائِزًا، نمبر18040، مصنف عبد الرزاق: بَاب طَلَاقُ السَّكْرَانِ نمبر 11419). عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ قَالَ: «هُوَ جَائِزٌ، إِنَّمَا هُوَ شَيْءٌ افْتَدَى بِهِ نَفْسَهُ» (مصنف ابن شيبه: نمبر 18041). عَنْ شُرَيْحٍ قَالَ: «الْقَيْدُ كُرْهٌ، وَالْوَعِيدُ كُرْهٌ، وَالسِّجْنُ كُرْهٌ» قَالَ عُمَرُ بْنُ الْخَطَّابِ: «لَيْسَ الرَّجُلُ أَمِينًا عَلَى نَفْسِهِ إِذَا أَجَعْتَهُ، أَوْ أَوْثَقْتَهُ، أَوْ ضَرَبْتَهُ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق: بَابٌ طَلَاقُ السَّكْرَانِ، نمبر 11424/11423). دليل الشافعي: وَإِلَّا مَنْ أُكْرِهَ وَقَلْبُهُ مُطْمَئِنٌّ بِالْإِيمَانِ وَلَكِنْ مَنْ شَرَحَ بِالْكُفْرِ صَدْرًا (القرآن، سورة النحل 106). وَعَنْ أَبِي ذَرٍّ الْغِفَارِيِّ قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «إِنَّ اللَّهَ قَدْ تَجَاوَزَ عَنْ أُمَّتِي الْخَطَأَ، وَالنِّسْيَانَ، وَمَا اسْتُكْرِهُوا عَلَيْهِ» (سنن ابن ماجه، بَابُ طَلَاقِ الْمُكْرَهِ وَالنَّاسِي، نمبر 2043). وَقَالَ ابْنُ عَبَّاسٍ: طَلَاقُ السَّكْرَانِ وَالْمُسْتَكْرَهِ لَيْسَ بِجَائِزٍ (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ الطَّلَاقِ فِي الْإِغْلَاقِ، نمبر 5269). عَنْ مُجَاهِدٍ قَالَ: «طَلَاقُ السَّكْرَانِ جَائِزٌ» (مصنف ابن أبي شيبه: نمبر 17957). عَنْ عَلِيٍّ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «رُفِعَ الْقَلَمُ عَنْ ثَلَاثَةٍ: عَنِ النَّائِمِ حَتَّى يَسْتَيْقِظَ، وَعَنِ الصَّبِيِّ حَتَّى يَحْتَلِمَ، وَعَنِ الْمَجْنُونِ حَتَّى يَعْقِلَ» (سنن أبي داود، بَابٌ فِي الْمَجْنُونِ يَسْرِقُ أَوْ يُصِيبُ حَدًّا، نمبر 4403). وَقَالَ عُثْمَانُ: «لَيْسَ لِمَجْنُونٍ وَلَا لِسَكْرَانَ طَلَاقٌ» (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ الطَّلَاقِ في الإغلاق، نمبر 5269). أصول: طلاق پر مجبور کیا گیا یا نشہ میں مست ہو کر طلاق دیا تو طلاق واقع ہو جائے گی، بر خلاف امام شافعی کے۔
Section: The divorce of the coerced and the intoxicated is effective. From Ibrahim: 'The divorce of the coerced is permissible.' From Ibrahim: 'It is permissible, it is something by which he ransoms himself.' From Shurayh: 'The shackle is coercion, the threat is coercion, the prison is coercion.' Umar ibn al-Khattab said: 'A man is not trustworthy over himself if you starve him, or tie him, or beat him.' Shafi'i's evidence: 'Except one who is coerced while his heart is at peace with faith, but whoever opens his breast to disbelief...' (Quran 16:106). And from Abu Dharr al-Ghifari: The Prophet said: 'Allah has pardoned my ummah for mistakes, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into doing.' Ibn Abbas said: 'The divorce of the intoxicated and the coerced is not permissible.' From Mujahid: 'The divorce of the intoxicated is permissible.' From Ali, from the Prophet: 'The pen has been raised from three: the sleeper until he wakes, the child until he reaches puberty, and the insane until he recovers.' Uthman said: 'There is no divorce for the insane or the intoxicated.' Principle: If one is coerced into divorce or gives divorce while intoxicated, the divorce occurs, contrary to Imam Shafi'i.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of divorce pronounced under coercion or intoxication. The Hanafi position, stated at the outset, is that such divorce is effective (wāqiʿ). The text provides proofs from the practice of the Salaf: Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Shurayh, and an athar from Umar. Then it presents the Shafi'i opposition: the Quranic verse on coercion (16:106) and the hadith of pardoning the coerced, and the opinion of Ibn Abbas and Uthman. The Hanafi response is implicit: these evidences apply to matters of faith and sin, not to the legal consequences of divorce, which are based on the explicit utterance. The principle (uṣūl) clarifies: coercion and intoxication do not remove legal capacity for divorce, unlike Shafi'i who equate the coerced and intoxicated with the insane. Thus, in Hanafi fiqh, the divorce is effective regardless of the state.

Key Terms
مُكْرَه (mukrah): One who is forced to pronounce divorce under threat of immediate harm.
سَكْرَان (sakrān): One who is intoxicated to the point of losing control but still conscious.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man is coerced into saying 'you are divorced' under threat of death, what is the ruling?
A) Divorce occurs and is effective.
B) Divorce does not occur because he was coerced.
C) Divorce is invalid because he did not intend it.
D) It depends on the method of coercion.

Key Takeaway
Coerced and intoxicated divorce is valid in Hanafi fiqh because the deliberate utterance of talaq, not intention, is the legal basis for its effectiveness.

Talaq — Divorce

Divorce by Intention, Gesture, and Conditional Pronouncement
States three rulings on divorce with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios where divorce is valid according to Hanafi fiqh and identify the evidence for each.

Core Idea
Divorce can be effected by intention, gesture of a mute, or a condition tied to future marriage.

This passage from al-Quduri presents three distinct rulings on the validity of divorce under specific circumstances. Each ruling is supported by prophetic practice, companion precedents, and juristic principles. Understanding these cases clarifies that divorce is not confined to explicit verbal pronouncements but includes situations where speech is impossible or a condition is made.

Guided Path
1
Examine divorce by intention
When a husband makes an ambiguous statement and later clarifies he intended divorce, it takes effect. Note the role of intention in ambiguous utterances.
Intention resolves ambiguity; the ruling applies when the wording is unclear.
2
Analyze gesture divorce for mute
The mute's clear gesture substitutes for speech out of necessity, supported by prophetic gesture analogy and juristic consensus.
Gesture is equivalent to speech in cases of necessity.
3
Evaluate conditional divorce before marriage
If a man attaches a divorce condition to a future marriage, it takes effect upon marriage, based on Umar's ruling.
The condition triggers the divorce when the marriage contract is concluded.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَقَعُ الطَّلَاقُ إِذَا قَالَ نَوَيْتُ بِهِ الطَّلَاقَ وَيَقَعُ طَلَاقُ الْأَخْرَسِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى النِّكَاحِ وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ النِّكَاحِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ لِأَجْنَبِيَّةٍ إِنْ تَزَوَّجْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ أَوْ كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ. عَنْ سَهْلٍ قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «وَأَنَا وَكَافِلُ الْيَتِيمِ فِي الْجَنَّةِ هَكَذَا» وَأَشَارَ بِالسَّبَّابَةِ وَالْوُسْطَى وَفَرَّجَ بَيْنَهُمَا شَيْئًا (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ اللَّعَانِ، نمبر 5304). وَقَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ: الْأَخْرَسُ إِذَا كَتَبَ الطَّلَاقَ بِيَدِهِ لَوَّمَهُ وَقَالَ حَمَّادٌ: الْأَخْرَسُ وَالْأَصَمُّ إِنْ قَالَ بِرَأْسِهِ جَازَ (صحيح البخاري، بَابُ اللَّعَانِ، نمبر 5300). عَنِ الزُّهْرِيِّ قَالَ: «إِذَا كَتَبَ إِلَيْهَا بِطَلَاقِهَا فَقَدْ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ عَلَيْهَا، فَإِنْ جَحَدَهَا اسْتُحْلِفَ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق، بَابُ الرَّجُلِ يَكْتُبُ إِلَى امْرَأَتِهِ بِطَلَاقِهَا، نمبر 11433). أَنَّ رَجُلًا أَتَى عُمَرَ بْنَ الْخَطَّابِ، فَقَالَ: كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا، فَقَالَ لَهُ عُمَرُ: «فَهُوَ كَمَا قُلْتَ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق، بَابُ الطَّلَاقِ قَبْلَ النِّكَاح، نمبر 11474). أصول: گونگے کا اشارہ بوقتِ ضرورت کلام کے درجہ میں ہو گا۔ أصول: نکاح کی شرط پر طلاق کو معلق کرے تو شرط پائے جانے پر جزا متحقق ہو گی یعنی طلاق ہو جائے گی۔
Divorce occurs when he says 'I intended divorce.' The divorce of the mute is valid by gesture. And if he attaches divorce to marriage, it occurs upon marriage, like if he says to a stranger: 'If I marry you, you are divorced,' or 'Every woman I marry is divorced.' From Sahl: The Prophet said: 'I and the caretaker of the orphan will be in Paradise like this' and he gestured with his index and middle fingers, separating them slightly. Ibrahim said: 'The mute, if he writes a divorce with his hand, he is blamed' and Hammad said: 'The mute and the deaf, if he gestures with his head, it is permissible.' From al-Zuhri: 'If he writes a divorce to her, the divorce occurs; if he denies it, he is made to take an oath.' A man came to Umar and said: 'Every woman I marry is thrice divorced.' Umar said: 'It is as you said.' Principle: The gesture of the mute, in times of necessity, is equivalent to speech. Principle: If he suspends divorce on a condition of marriage, when the condition occurs, the consequence realizes, i.e., divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents three distinct rulings with supporting evidence. First, divorce occurs if the husband clarifies that he intended divorce when making an ambiguous statement; this establishes that intention matters when the wording is unclear. Second, the divorce of a mute person is valid through clear gesture (e.g., nodding or writing), as gesture substitutes for speech when speech is impossible. Evidence includes the Prophet's own gesture as an analogy (hadith of orphan) and statements of Ibrahim and Hammad. Third, if a man says to a stranger 'If I marry you, you are divorced' or any similar condition, the divorce takes effect immediately upon marriage. This is based on the precedent of Caliph Umar (r.a.). The appended principles summarise: the mute's gesture is legally equivalent to speech out of necessity, and conditional divorce tied to a future marriage becomes effective when the marriage occurs.

Key Terms
إِشَارَة (ishārah): Gestural communication used by a mute person to effect divorce.
أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى النِّكَاحِ (aḍāfa al-ṭalāqa ilā al-nikāḥ): Attaching a divorce condition to a future marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Caliph Umar say to the man who said 'Every woman I marry is thrice divorced'?
A) 'You must not do that.'
B) 'It is as you said.'
C) 'Divorce before marriage is invalid.'
D) 'You must repeat it after marriage.'

Key Takeaway
Intention, necessity, and conditionality can make divorce valid even without direct verbal pronouncement.

Divorce of a Mute Person by Sign
Ruling and proofs that a mute's divorce by sign is valid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a mute's divorce by sign, identify the proofs (hadith, tabi'i opinions), and apply the principle that a mute's sign is equivalent to speech in necessity.

Core Idea
A mute's deliberate sign for divorce is valid because necessity elevates it to the status of speech.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position that a mute person's divorce is valid when expressed through clear signs. The ruling is based on the Qur'anic principle of facilitating necessities, supported by the Prophet's own use of gesture in a hadith and the practice of the early tabi'un. Understanding this ruling requires connecting the legal maxim 'necessity permits the exceptional' to the specific case of the mute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subject
Locate the exact ruling in the matn: 'Divorce of a mute occurs by sign.' Note that the subject is a person who cannot speak (al-akhras) and the act is talaq pronounced by deliberate gesture.
The ruling is a statement of validity: the divorce takes effect.
2
Analyze the hadith proof
Read the hadith where the Prophet used hand gesture to indicate closeness. Understand that this shows intentional signs are considered communicative in Shariah. The wajh al-dalalah (point of evidence) is that the Prophet's action establishes that gestures can convey legal meaning.
The hadith is from Sahih al-Bukhari, so it is qati al-thubut but zanni al-dalalah.
3
Examine the tabi'i opinions
Consider the statements of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Hammad: they accept written or gestured divorce from a mute. Also, al-Zuhri's acceptance of written divorce reinforces that non-verbal expression is valid when speech is impossible.
These are not primary proofs but support the Hanafi position and show early practice.
4
Extract the usul principle
The text concludes: 'The sign of a mute, at the time of necessity, is at the level of speech.' This is the key principle—necessity (darurah) transforms the ruling of sign to that of speech.
This is a manifestation of the broader maxim: al-daruratu tubihu al-mahzurat.
5
Consider cross-madhhab dispute
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may require verbal utterance or written text for divorce. The Hanafi school responds by emphasizing the necessity and the communicative nature of clear signs, supported by the hadith.
The practical difference: Hanafi accepts a nod or gesture; others may not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَقَعُ طَلَاقُ الْأَخْرَسِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ
وجه: (ا) الحديث لثبوت وَيَقَعُ طَلَاقُ الْأَخْرَسِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ عَنْ سَهْلٍ قَالَ رَسُولُ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «وَأَنَا وَكَافِلُ الْيَتِيمِ فِي الْجَنَّةِ هَكَذَا» وَأَشَارَ بِالسَّبَّابَةِ وَالْوُسْطَى وَفَرَّجَ بَيْنَهُمَا شَيْئًا. (صحيح البخاري بَابُ اللَّعَانِ، نمبر 5304)
وجه: (۲) قول التابعي لثبوت وَيَقَعُ طَلَاقُ الْأَخْرَسِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ وَقَالَ إِبْرَاهِيمُ: الْأَخْرَسُ إِذَا كَتَبَ الطَّلَاقَ بِيَدِهِ لَوْمَهُ، وَقَالَ حَمَّادٌ: الْأَخْرَسُ وَالْأَصَمُّ إِنْ قَالَ بِرَأْسِهِ جَازَ. (بخاري بَابُ اللَّعَانِ، 5300)
وجه: (۳) قول التابعي لثبوت وَيَقَعُ طَلَاقُ الْأَخْرَسِ بِالْإِشَارَةِ عَنِ الزُّهْرِيِّ قَالَ: «إِذَا كَتَبَ إِلَيْهَا بِطَلَاقِهَا فَقَدْ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ عَلَيْهَا، فَإِنْ جَحَدَهَا اسْتُحْلِفَ» (مصنف عبدالرزاق، بَابُ الرَّجُلِ يَكْتُبُ إِلَى امْرَأَتِهِ بِطَلَاقِهَا، نمبر 11433)
اصول: گونگے کا اشارہ بوقتِ ضرورت کلام کے درجہ میں ہو گا۔
And the divorce of a mute person occurs by sign. Face (1): The hadith for establishing that the divorce of a mute occurs by sign: from Sahl that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'I and the caretaker of an orphan will be in Paradise like this' and he pointed with his index and middle fingers and separated them slightly. (Sahih al-Bukhari, Book of Cursing, no. 5304) Face (2): The saying of a Tabi'i for establishing that the divorce of a mute occurs by sign: Ibrahim said: 'The mute, if he writes divorce with his hand, it is blameworthy' and Hammad said: 'The mute and the deaf, if he indicates with his head, it is valid.' (Bukhari, Book of Cursing, 5300) Face (3): The saying of a Tabi'i for establishing that the divorce of a mute occurs by sign: from al-Zuhri who said: 'If he writes to her of her divorce, then divorce has occurred upon her, and if he denies it, he is made to take an oath.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter of a man writing to his wife about her divorce, no. 11433) Principle: The sign of a mute, at the time of necessity, is at the level of speech.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling that a mute person's divorce is valid through sign language, along with its proofs. The ruling is from the Quduri matn. The author then cites three proofs: (1) a hadith where the Prophet used a hand gesture to indicate being close to someone, establishing that intentional gestures are legally recognized; (2) the opinion of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i and Hammad that a mute's written or gestured divorce is valid; and (3) the practice of al-Zuhri accepting a written divorce from a mute. The Urdu principle states that a mute's sign, when necessary, stands in place of speech.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who cannot speak due to a congenital or acquired condition.
الإشارة (al-isharah): A deliberate gesture that clearly indicates intention, such as nodding or pointing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a mute person divorcing his wife by sign?
A) It is invalid because divorce requires speech.
B) It is valid because his sign is equivalent to speech when necessary.
C) It is valid only if written.
D) It is makruh.

Key Takeaway
A mute's divorce by deliberate sign is valid in the Hanafi school because necessity makes the sign equivalent to speech.

Divorce of the Coerced and Drunk Person
Ruling with inter-madhhab dispute on validity of coerced and drunk divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the divorce of a coerced and drunk person is valid, identify the Shafi'i counter-evidence (hadith 'the pen is lifted' and Uthman's statement), and explain the Hanafi response based on accountability for self-inflicted intoxication and the nature of coercion.

Core Idea
A drunk's self-inflicted intoxication does not excuse divorce; coercion does not remove accountability.

This passage presents a classical Hanafi ruling that the divorces pronounced by a coerced person (al-mukrah) and a drunk person (al-sakran) are valid and effective. This contrasts with the Shafi'i position, which holds such divorces invalid. You will explore the key evidence from both sides and understand why the Hanafi school holds the drunk and coerced person accountable.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the explicit ruling: 'wa talaq al-mukrahi wa al-sakrani waqi'' — the divorce of the coerced and the drunk is valid.
This is the core of the matn.
2
Identify the Shafi'i proofs
Note two proofs cited: the hadith 'the pen is lifted from three' (sleeper, child, insane) and the saying of Uthman 'no divorce for an insane nor a drunk'.
The opponent's dalil is clearly listed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response (implied)
Understand that the Hanafi school distinguishes the drunk from the insane: the drunk's intoxication is self-inflicted, so he is accountable. For the coerced, the threat does not negate intent entirely unless reason is lost.
The commentator's reasoning fills the gap.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: in Hanafi law, a drunk or coerced husband's divorce is effective, affecting the wife's ability to remarry and maintenance rights.
This shows why the dispute matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَطَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ وَالسَّكْرَانِ وَاقِعٌ
وجه: (۲) قول التابعي لثبوت وَطَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ وَالسَّكْرَانِ وَاقِعٌ عَنْ مُجَاهِدٍ قَالَ: «طَلَاقُ السَّكْرَانِ جَائِزٌ» (مصنف ابن أبي شيبة : مَنْ أَجَازَ طَلَاقَ السَّكْرَانِ، نمبر 17957)
وجه: (۱) دليل الشافعي الحديث لثبوت وَطَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ عَنْ عَلِيٍّ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «رُفِعَ الْقَلَمُ عَنْ ثَلَاثَةٍ: عَنِ النَّائِمِ حَتَّى يَسْتَيْقِظَ، وَعَنِ الصَّبِيِّ حَتَّى يَحْتَلِمَ، وَعَنِ الْمَجْنُونِ حَتَّى يَعْقِلَ» (سنن أبي داود، بَابٌ فِي الْمَجْنُونِ يَسْرِقُ أَوْ يُصِيبُ حَدًّا، نمبر 4403)
وجه: (۲) دليل الشافعي قول الصحابي لثبوت وَطَلَاقُ الْمُكْرَهِ وَقَالَ عُثْمَانُ: «لَيْسَ لِمَجْنُونٍ وَلَا لِسَكْرَانَ طَلَاقٌ» (صحيح البخاري بَابُ الطَّلَاقِ في الإغلاق، نمبر 5269)
And the divorce of the coerced and the drunk is valid. Face (2): The saying of a Tabi'i for establishing that the divorce of the coerced and the drunk is valid: from Mujahid who said: 'The divorce of a drunk person is permissible.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba: Whoever allowed divorce of the drunk, no. 17957) Face (1): The proof of al-Shafi'i from the hadith for establishing the divorce of the coerced: from Ali from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'The pen is lifted from three: from the sleeper until he wakes, from the child until he reaches puberty, and from the insane until he regains sanity.' (Sunan Abi Dawud, Chapter on the insane who steals or commits a hadd, no. 4403) Face (2): The proof of al-Shafi'i from the saying of a Companion for establishing the divorce of the coerced: Uthman said: 'There is no divorce for an insane person nor for a drunk.' (Sahih al-Bukhari, Chapter of divorce in duress, no. 5269)
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that the divorce of a coerced person and a drunk person is valid, and then provides the Shafi'i counter-evidence. The Hanafi school holds that a drunk person is legally responsible for divorce because intoxication is a result of his own sin, and coercion does not remove intent in the absence of a genuine loss of reason. The Shafi'i school relies on the hadith 'the pen is lifted' for three categories, arguing that a drunk and coerced person lack free will. They also cite Uthman's statement. The Hanafi response (not fully quoted here) distinguishes that a drunk is not insane and a coerced person still intends the action, though under duress.

Key Terms
المكره (al-mukrah): A person who is forced to do something against his will under threat.
السكران (al-sakran): A person who has consumed an intoxicant and lost some level of consciousness but not completely insane.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The drunk person is considered sane like a sober person.
B) The drunk person is accountable because his intoxication is self-inflicted.
C) The hadith 'the pen is lifted' explicitly excludes the drunk.
D) The drunk person's divorce is invalid unless he confirms it later.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that divorce pronounced by a coerced or drunk person is valid and effective, unlike the Shafi'i school which considers it invalid.

Suspending Divorce upon Marriage: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling and dispute on suspending divorce upon future marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that a conditional divorce pronounced before marriage is valid, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce before marriage is valid in Hanafi law because the condition is an effective cause upon marriage.

This passage addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute: Can a man divorce a woman he has not yet married? The Hanafi school says yes, provided the divorce is made conditional upon the marriage itself. The Shafi'i school objects on the grounds that one cannot divorce what one does not own. Let us examine the proofs and their implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: If a man adds divorce to marriage, the divorce occurs immediately after marriage. Example: 'If I marry you, you are divorced.'
This is the primary ruling from the matn.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
The Shafi'i school cites the hadith: 'No divorce except in that which you own.' They argue that at the time of pronouncement, the man does not own the woman, so the divorce is invalid.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Hanafis counter with the practice of Umar ibn al-Khattab, who enforced such a condition, and the saying of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that if a specific woman or tribe is designated, the condition is valid. They interpret the hadith as referring to absolute ownership, not conditional future ownership.
The Hanafi response relies on qawl al-sahabi and qawl al-tabi'i.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi, the wife is divorced upon marriage. Under Shafi'i, the condition is void and the marriage remains intact. This is the practical difference.
Mufta bihi in Hanafi school is the validity of the conditional divorce.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى النِّكَاحِ وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ النِّكَاحِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ لِأَجْنَبِيَّةٍ: إِنْ تَزَوَّجْتُكِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ، أَوْ كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ.
وجه: (۱) دليل الشافعي الحديث لثبوت وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى النِّكَاحِ عَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ عَنْ أَبِيهِ عَنْ جَدِّهِ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «لَا طَلَاقَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ، وَلَا عِتْقَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ، وَلَا بَيْعَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ»، زَادَ ابْنُ الصَّبَّاحِ: «وَلَا وَفَاءَ نَذْرٍ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ» (أبو داود: باب في الطلاق قبل النكاح، نمبر 2190)
وجه: (۱) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى النِّكَاحِ أَنَّ رَجُلًا أَتَى عُمَرَ بْنَ الْخَطَّابِ فَقَالَ: كُلُّ امْرَأَةٍ أَتَزَوَّجُهَا فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ ثَلَاثًا، فَقَالَ لَهُ عُمَرُ: «فَهُوَ كَمَا قُلْتَ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق، بَابُ الطَّلَاقِ قَبْلَ النِّكَاح، نمبر 11474)
وجه: (۲) قول التابعي لثبوت وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى النِّكَاحِ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ قَالَ: «إِذَا وَفَّتَ امْرَأَةً أَوْ قَبِيلَةً جَازَ، وَإِذَا عَمَّ كُلَّ امْرَأَةٍ فَلَيْسَ بِشَيْءٍ» (مصنف عبد الرزاق، بَابُ الطَّلَاقِ قَبْلَ النِّكَاحِ، نمبر 11471)
اصول: نکاح کی شرط پر طلاق کو معلق کرے تو شرط پائے جانے پر جزا متحقق ہو گی یعنی طلاق ہو جائے گی۔
And if he adds divorce to marriage, it occurs immediately after marriage, like if he says to a foreign woman: 'If I marry you, you are divorced', or 'Every woman I marry is divorced'. Face (1): The proof of al-Shafi'i from the hadith for establishing that adding divorce to marriage (is invalid): from Amr ibn Shu'ayb from his father from his grandfather that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'There is no divorce except in that which you own, and no emancipation except in that which you own, and no sale except in that which you own.' Ibn al-Sabbah added: 'And no fulfillment of a vow except in that which you own.' (Abu Dawud: Chapter on divorce before marriage, no. 2190) Face (1): The saying of a Companion for establishing adding divorce to marriage: A man came to Umar ibn al-Khattab and said: 'Every woman I marry is thrice divorced.' Umar said to him: 'It is as you said.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter of divorce before marriage, no. 11474) Face (2): The saying of a Tabi'i for establishing adding divorce to marriage: from Ibrahim who said: 'If he specifies a woman or a tribe, it is valid; if he generalizes every woman, it is nothing.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Chapter of divorce before marriage, no. 11471) Principle: If he suspends divorce on the condition of marriage, upon the occurrence of the condition, the consequence is realized, i.e., divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage gives the Hanafi ruling that a conditional divorce tied to marriage takes effect immediately upon marriage. The Shafi'i school objects using the hadith 'no divorce except in that which you own', arguing that one cannot divorce a woman he does not own at the time of pronouncement. The Hanafi school counters with the practice of Umar who enforced such a condition, and the opinion of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that if a specific woman is designated, the divorce is valid. The Hanafi position is that the condition is effectively an oath (yamin) and becomes binding upon marriage. The Urdu principle confirms that a conditional divorce (ta'liq) triggers upon fulfillment of the condition.

Key Terms
أضاف الطلاق (adafa al-talaq): To make divorce conditional upon a future event, such as marriage or another condition.
النكاح (al-nikah): Marriage contract; the condition upon which divorce is suspended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says to a woman 'If I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her, what happens?
A) The statement is void because she was not his wife at the time.
B) She is divorced immediately upon marriage.
C) He must pronounce divorce again after marriage.
D) The marriage is invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a conditional divorce tied to marriage is a valid ta'liq and takes effect upon marriage, a position supported by Companion practice and Tabi'i opinion.

The Validity of Conditional Divorce (Ta'liq al-Talaq)
Ruling and proof that conditional divorce takes effect upon condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditional divorce and explain why the Shafi'i counter-argument based on the hadith 'No divorce except in that which you own' is rejected.

Core Idea
A divorce suspended on a future condition takes effect only when the condition occurs; the marriage remains valid in the interim.

This passage addresses a common scenario: a husband says, 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' Does this divorce take effect immediately, or only upon entry? The Hanafi school rules that the divorce is suspended—it occurs only when the condition is fulfilled. This lesson unpacks the evidence, including the hadith cited by the Shafi'i school, and shows how the Hanafis interpret it differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the first line: 'If he adds divorce to a condition, it occurs immediately upon the condition.' This is the Hanafi position: the divorce is not immediate but becomes effective instantly when the condition is met.
Contrast with immediate divorce (munajjaz).
2
Examine the Shafi'i Objection
The Shafi'i school uses the hadith 'No divorce except in that which you own' to argue that a conditional divorce is invalid because at the time of utterance the husband does not 'own' the divorce in the sense of immediate effect.
The hadith is quoted as Face (1) in the source.
3
Analyse the Hanafi Response
Hanafis interpret 'own' as the right to divorce, which the husband certainly has. The condition merely delays the effect. They support this with the opinion of al-Hasan al-Basri (Face 2), who explicitly states that such a pronouncement is not divorce until the condition occurs, and the marriage remains valid in the meantime.
Al-Hasan's statement confirms that interim marital rights (intercourse, inheritance) continue.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: Under Hanafi, the wife remains married until the condition occurs; under Shafi'i, the statement may be treated as a vow or oath, not a divorce, so no divorce ever takes effect.
This affects the wife's status and future remarriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى شَرْطٍ وَقَعَ عَقِيبَ الشَّرْطِ مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ: إِنْ دَخَلْتِ الدَّارَ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ.
وجه: (ا) الحديث لثبوت وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى شَرْطٍ عَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ عَنْ أَبِيهِ عَنْ جَدِّهِ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «لَا طَلَاقَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ، وَلَا عِتْقَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ، وَلَا بَيْعَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ»، زَادَ ابْنُ الصَّبَّاحِ: «وَلَا وَفَاءَ نَذْرٍ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ» (أبو داود: باب في الطلاق قبل النكاح، نمبر 2190)
وجه: (۲) قول التابعي لثبوت وَإِذَا أَضَافَ الطَّلَاقَ إِلَى شَرْطٍ عَنِ الْحَسَنِ قَالَ: «إِذَا قَالَ: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ إِذَا كَانَ كَذَا وَكَذَا الْأَمْرُ لَا يَدْرِي أَيَكُونُ أَمْ لَا، فَلَيْسَ بِطَلَاقٍ حَتَّى يَكُونَ ذَلِكَ، وَلَهُ أَنْ يَطَأَهَا فِيمَا بَيْنَ ذَلِكَ، وَإِنْ مَاتَ قَبْلَ مَا أَجَّلَ تَوَارَثْنَا» (مصنف عبدالرزاق: باب الطلاق إلى أجل، نمبر 11315)
اصول: طلاق کو شرط پر معلق کیا تو شرط پائے جانے پر طلاق واقع ہو گی۔
And if he adds divorce to a condition, it occurs immediately upon the condition, like if he says to his wife: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced.' Face (1): The hadith for establishing that adding divorce to a condition (is invalid): from Amr ibn Shu'ayb... (same hadith as above). Face (2): The saying of a Tabi'i for establishing that adding divorce to a condition: from al-Hasan who said: 'If he says: "You are divorced if such and such happens" – the matter is unknown whether it will happen or not – it is not divorce until that occurs, and he may have intercourse with her in the meantime, and if he dies before the appointed time, they inherit from each other.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq: Chapter of divorce to a term, no. 11315) Principle: If divorce is suspended on a condition, then upon the occurrence of the condition, divorce occurs.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage gives the ruling that a conditional divorce suspended on a future event (like entering a house) takes effect when the condition is fulfilled. The Shafi'i school again uses the hadith 'no divorce except in that which you own' to argue that a conditional divorce is not valid if pronounced without immediate effect. The Hanafi school responds by citing al-Hasan al-Basri's opinion that such a conditional pronouncement is not immediately effective but becomes effective when the condition occurs, and in the interim the marriage remains valid (he may have intercourse, they inherit). This is the Hanafi position of ta'liq al-talaq, which is binding when the condition is met.

Key Terms
الشرط (al-shart): A future event upon which the divorce is made conditional.
تعليق الطلاق (ta'liq al-talaq): Suspending divorce upon a condition; a common practice in Hanafi fiqh.
طلاق معلق (talaq mu'allaq): A divorce that is suspended on a future condition; also called ta'liq.
ملك (milk): The right of ownership; in context, the husband's right to divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A conditional divorce (ta'liq) is valid in the Hanafi school; it takes effect only when the specified condition occurs, and the marriage remains intact in the interim.

Talaq — Divorce
beginner
6 min
Condition for Valid Addition of Divorce
General rule with proof for valid addition of divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid suspended divorce and apply it to scenarios involving current or future marriage.

Core Idea
A suspended divorce is valid only if the speaker owns the right to divorce or attaches it to a future ownership.

Divorce can be pronounced conditionally (suspended) on a future event. But not every conditional pronouncement is valid. The Hanafi school sets a clear condition: the speaker must either already be a husband or link the divorce to a future marriage. This ensures that the right to divorce is tied to ownership of the wife at the time the condition is fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'إضافة الطلاق' (adding/suspending divorce) to a condition. The ruling: it is valid only if the speaker is an owner (مالك) or adds it to ownership.
Ownership here means being a husband who has the right to divorce.
2
Extract the condition
The condition is that the speaker (الخالف) must be either a current owner (i.e., husband) or a future owner (by adding to a future marriage).
‘Adding to ownership’ means saying ‘if I marry you, you are divorced.’
3
Locate the proof
The hadith: 'لا طلاق إلا فيما تملك' (no divorce except in what you own). The Hanafi interpretation extends this to future ownership via conditional statements.
The hadith is from Sunan Abi Dawud and is zanni in thubut and dalala.
4
Apply the ruling
e.g., a stranger says ‘if you marry so-and-so, she is divorced’ – invalid because no ownership connection. A man says ‘if I marry you, you are divorced’ – valid because future ownership.
The key is the link between the speaker and the woman at the time of condition fulfillment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا يَصِحُّ إِضَافَةُ الطَّلَاقِ إِلَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ الْخَالِفُ مَالِكًا أَوْ يُضِيفَهُ إِلَى مِلْكِ.
وجه: (ا) الحديث لثبوت وَلَا يَصِحُّ إِضَافَةُ الطَّلَاقِ عَنْ عَمْرِو بْنِ شُعَيْبٍ عَنْ أَبِيهِ عَنْ جَدِّهِ أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «لَا طَلَاقَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ، وَلَا عِتْقَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ، وَلَا بَيْعَ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ»، زَادَ ابْنُ الصَّبَّاحِ: «وَلَا وَفَاءَ نَذْرٍ إِلَّا فِيمَا تَمْلِكُ» (أبو داود: باب في الطلاق قبل النكاح، نمبر 2190)
And the addition of divorce is not valid unless the one who makes it is an owner or he adds it to ownership. Face (1): The hadith for establishing that addition of divorce is not valid: from Amr ibn Shu'ayb... (same hadith).
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage lays down a fundamental condition for any suspended or conditional divorce: the person must either already own the right to divorce (i.e., be a husband) or suspend it upon a future ownership (like 'if I marry you'). The hadith 'no divorce except in that which you own' is used as proof, but the Hanafi school interprets 'ownership' broadly: a husband owns the right to divorce his current wife, and a man can condition divorce upon future marriage because that condition, when fulfilled, gives him ownership. Thus, the ruling is that adding divorce is invalid if done by someone without any ownership connection (e.g., a stranger saying 'if you marry so-and-so, she is divorced'), but valid if the speaker is or will become the husband.

Key Terms
إضافة الطلاق (idafat al-talaq): The act of suspending divorce on a condition or future event.
الخالف (al-khalif): The person who makes the conditional statement (the one adding divorce).
مالك (malik): Owner of the right to divorce (i.e., a husband) or someone who will own (e.g., future husband).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what condition is the addition (suspension) of divorce valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Only if the speaker is already married at the time.
B) Only if the speaker is an owner or adds it to future ownership.
C) Only if the speaker is a male.
D) Only if pronounced in the presence of witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Addition of divorce is valid only if the speaker is an owner (husband) or attaches it to a future ownership.

Conditional Divorce and the Repetition Rule of Kullamā
Ruling with proof on conditional divorce words and the repetition effect of kullamā

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the words used for conditional divorce, explain the rule that only kullamā triggers repeated divorces, and identify the evidence from Quran and Hadith.

Core Idea
Only the word 'kullamā' triggers repeated divorces; all other condition words fulfill the oath once.

Conditional divorce (talāq ta‘līqī) is a common practice where a husband makes divorce contingent on a future event. The Hanafi school distinguishes between general condition words and the specific word 'kullamā'. This lesson explains the ruling, its practical application, and the proofs from the sacred texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition words
List the Arabic words used to create conditional divorce: 'in', 'idhā', 'idhā mā', 'kull', 'kullamā', 'matā', 'matā mā'.
These are the standard particles for conditions in Arabic.
2
Apply the general rule
All these words except kullamā cause the oath to be fulfilled and the divorce to occur only once, even if the condition repeats.
Example: 'If you enter the house, you are divorced' → one divorce on first entry.
3
Apply the exception for kullamā
When kullamā (whenever/every time) is used, each occurrence of the condition triggers a new divorce, up to a maximum of three.
After three divorces, she becomes unlawful to him (unless she marries another and is divorced).
4
Consider post-remarriage scenario
If the husband remarries her after three divorces, further occurrences of the condition do not cause divorce because the marriage is invalid without tahlīl.
The effect of kullamā is limited to the first marriage.
5
Recognize the proofs
The hadith 'No divorce before marriage' establishes the need for a valid marriage. Quranic verses (e.g., 65:1) show condition words in divine speech. The verse 4:56 and the statement of al-Ḥakam demonstrate the repetitive meaning of kullamā.
These proofs are from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَلْفَاظُ الشَّرْطِ إِنْ وَإِذَا وَإِذَا مَا وَكُلٌّ وَكُلَّمَا وَمَتَى وَمَتَى مَا : وَكُلُّ هَذِهِ الشُّرُوطِ إِذَا وُجِدَتْ الْحَلَّتْ الْيَمِينُ إلَّا فِي كُلَّمَا فَإِنَّ الطَّلَاقَ يَتَكَرَّرُ بِتَكَرُّرِ الشَّرْطِ حَتَّى يَقَعَ ثَلَاثَ تَطْلِيقَاتِ ال فَإِنْ تَزَوَّجَهَا بَعْدَ ذَلِكَ وَتَكَرَّرَ الشَّرْطُ لَمْ يَقَعْ شَيْءٌ وَزَوَالُ الْمِلْكِ بَعْدَ الْيَمِينِ لَا يُبْطِلُهَا فَإِنْ وجِدَ الشَّرْطُ وَهِيَ فِي مِلْكِهِ الْحَلَّتْ الْيَمِينُ وَوَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ وَإِنْ وُجِدَ فِي غَيْرِ مِلْكِهِ الْحَلَّتْ الْيَمِينُ وَلَمْ يَقَعُ شَيْءٌ. وجه: (٢)الحديث لثبوت وَلَا يَصِحُ إِضَافَةُ الطَّلَاقِ عَنْ عَلِيِّ بْنِ أَبِي طَالِبٍ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم ، قَالَ: «لَا طَلَاقَ قَبْلَ النِّكَاحِ» سنن ابن ماجه شريف : (۱۷) باب لا طلاق قبل النكاح، 2049) دوجه: (1) الآية لثبوت وَأَلْفَاظُ الشَّرْطِ إِنْ وَإِذَا ا ﴿وَإِن تَعُدُّوا نِعْمَتَ اللَّهِ لَا تُحْصُوهَا إِنَّ الْإِنسَانَ لَظَلُومٌ كَفَّارُ﴾ (القرآن،سورة ابراهيم 14، الآية، نمبر 34) وجه (۲) الآية لثبوت وَأَلْفَاظُ الشَّرْطِ إِنَّ وَإِذَا ا ﴿يَتأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ إِذَا طَلَّقْتُمُ النِّسَاءَ فَطَلِقُوهُنَّ لِعِدَّتِهِنَّ وَأَحْصُواْ الْعِدَّةَ ﴾ (القرآن،سورة الطلاق 65، الآية، نمبر 1) ١٠ وجه (1) الآية لثبوت إلا في كُلَّمَا فَإِنَّ الطَّلَاقَ يَتَكَرَّرُ كُلَّمَا نَضِجَتْ جُلُودُهُم بَدَّلْتَهُمْ جُلُودًا غَيْرَهَا لِيَذُوقُواْ الْعَذَابَ (القرآن، سورة النساء 56، الآية، نمبر 56) وجه (۲) قول التابعى لثبوت إلا في كُلَّمَا فَإِنَّ الطَّلَاقَ يَتَكَرُرُ سَأَلْتُ الْحَكَمَ، وَحَمَّادًا، عَنْ رَجُلٍ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ: أَنْتِ طَالِقٌ كُلَّمَا شِئْتِ، قَالَ الْحَكَمُ: «كُلَّمَا شَاءَتْ، فَهِيَ طَالِقٌ» (مصنف ابن شيبه في رجل قال لامرأته : أنت طالق كلما شئت، نمبر 19091)
اصول: کلما کے علاوہ تمام الفاظ شرط سے ایک ہی بار قسم پوری ہو گی اور ایک ہی طلاق ہو گی۔
اصول: كُلَّما کا تقاضا ملک اول تک رہتا ہے، اس کے بعد ختم ہو جاتا ہے۔
The words of condition are: 'in', 'idhā', 'idhā mā', 'kull', 'kullamā', 'matā', 'matā mā'. All of these conditions, when they occur, fulfill the oath except in kullamā, for divorce repeats with the repetition of the condition until three divorces occur. If he marries her after that and the condition repeats, nothing occurs. The removal of ownership after the oath does not nullify it. If the condition is found while she is in his ownership, the oath is fulfilled and divorce occurs. If it is found while she is not in his ownership, the oath is fulfilled and nothing occurs. Evidence: (2) The hadith for establishing that it is not valid to attribute divorce: from 'Alī ibn Abī Tālib from the Prophet (PBUH) who said: 'No divorce before marriage.' (Sunan Ibn Mājah, chapter: No Divorce Before Nikah, no. 2049) Evidence: (1) The verse for establishing the words of condition 'in' and 'idhā': 'And if you count the favors of Allah, you will not enumerate them...' (Quran, Surah Ibrahim 14:34) Evidence: (2) The verse for establishing the words of condition 'in' and 'idhā': 'O Prophet, when you divorce women, divorce them for their waiting period...' (Quran, Surah At-Talaq 65:1) Evidence: (1) The verse for establishing the exception of kullamā that divorce repeats: 'Whenever their skins are roasted, We replace them with other skins...' (Quran, Surah An-Nisa 4:56) Evidence: (2) The saying of the tābi'ī for establishing the exception of kullamā that divorce repeats: I asked al-Ḥakam and Ḥammād about a man who said to his wife: 'You are divorced whenever you wish.' Al-Ḥakam said: 'Whenever she wishes, she is divorced.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 19091) Principle: All condition words except kullamā fulfill the oath once and cause one divorce. Principle: The requirement of kullamā remains until the first ownership ends, after which it ceases.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on conditional divorce (talāq ta‘līqī) in the Hanafi school. It lists the Arabic words used to make divorce conditional: 'in' (if), 'idhā' (when), 'idhā mā' (whenever), 'kull' (every), 'kullamā' (whenever/every time), 'matā' (when), 'matā mā' (whenever). The rule is that all these words, except kullamā, fulfill the oath (i.e., cause the divorce to occur) only once upon the first occurrence of the condition. For example, if a husband says 'If you enter the house, you are divorced,' and she enters, one divorce occurs. If she enters again, no additional divorce occurs because the oath has already been fulfilled. However, with 'kullamā,' each time the condition occurs, a new divorce takes place, up to a maximum of three divorces. This is because 'kullamā' implies repetition. The text further clarifies that if the husband divorces his wife thrice via kullamā, then later remarries her, the condition (if repeated) will not cause further divorce because after three divorces, she becomes unlawful to him unless she marries another man (tahlīl). The ruling also states that the expiration of marriage (e.g., through divorce) after the oath does not invalidate the conditional oath; if the condition subsequently occurs while she is still in his marriage (i.e., before the divorce is final), the divorce takes effect. But if the condition occurs after she is no longer his wife (e.g., after the waiting period), the oath is fulfilled but no divorce occurs because there is no marriage to dissolve. The book then cites proofs: the hadith 'No divorce before marriage' from Ali (RA) establishes that conditional divorce is only valid if pronounced within a valid marriage; the Quranic verses mentioning 'in' and 'idhā' show these words are used in divine speech, affirming their linguistic validity; and the verse 'kullamā nadijat julūduhum' demonstrates the repetitive meaning of kullamā, as does the statement of the early scholar al-Ḥakam. Finally, two Urdu principles summarize: all condition words except kullamā cause one divorce; and the effect of kullamā persists only during the first marriage.

Key Terms
كُلَّمَا (kullamā): A conditional word meaning 'whenever' or 'every time'; in this context, it causes divorce to repeat with each occurrence of the condition.
الشَّرْط (al-sharṭ): The condition upon which the divorce is contingent; in this context, the event that triggers the fulfillment of the oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife: 'You are divorced whenever you eat fish.' She eats fish three times, then they divorce. After her waiting period, they remarry, and she eats fish again. How many divorces occur in total?
A) Three divorces (all from the first marriage)
B) Four divorces (three from first marriage, one from second)
C) One divorce from the first marriage only
D) No divorce because the word is not kullamā

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only the word 'kullamā' causes repeated divorces; all other condition words fulfill the oath once, and the effect of kullamā ends with the first marriage.
