Dispute over Condition Occurrence and Her Word on Private Matters
Ruling on burden of proof in dispute over divorce condition occurrence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on burden of proof when husband and wife dispute a divorce condition, distinguishing between public and private conditions, and state the evidential exception.

Core Idea
The husband's word is default, but the wife's word is accepted on matters only she can know.

When a conditional divorce is pronounced, disputes may arise over whether the condition actually occurred. This lesson addresses a key procedural rule in Hanafi fiqh: who bears the burden of proof and whose statement is accepted by default. The general rule is that the husband's denial is accepted, but if the condition is something only the wife can verify—like her own menstruation—her word is taken on that matter. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying evidence rules in marriage disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed condition
Determine whether the condition is public (e.g., going to a place) or private (only known to the wife, e.g., menstruation). This classification determines who is believed.
If the condition is public, the husband's denial is accepted unless the wife brings evidence. If private, the wife's claim is accepted.
2
Apply the default rule
For public conditions, the husband is the denier; his word is taken. The hadith 'al-bayyinatu 'alā man idda‘ā wa al-yamīnu 'alā man ankara' — burden of proof on the claimant, oath on the denier — supports this.
The wife is the claimant if she asserts the condition occurred. She must bring evidence (two male witnesses or one male and two females).
3
Recognize the exception for private matters
If the condition is inherently private (e.g., menstruation), the wife's word is accepted regarding herself, as only she can know it. No evidence is required from her.
This exception applies only to herself; it does not extend to others like a co-wife.
4
Apply the ruling to examples
Work through the given examples: husband says 'When you menstruate, you are divorced' — wife's claim of menstruation is accepted. If the condition involves another woman, her claim does not affect the other.
The example shows that the exception is personal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا اخْتَلَفَا فِي وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الزَّوْجِ إِلَّا أَنْ تُقِيمَ الْمَرْأَةُ بَيِّنَةً فَإِنْ كَانَ الشَّرْطُ لا يُعْلَمُ إِلَّا مِنْ جِهَتِهَا فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُهَا فِي حَق نَفْسِهَا مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ إِذَا حِضْت فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ فَقَالَتْ قَدْ حِضْت طَلُقَتْ وَإِذَا قَالَ إِنْ حِضْتَ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَفُلَانَةُ مَعَكَ فَقَالَتْ حِضْتَ طَلُقَتْ هِيَ وَلَمْ تَطْلُقْ فُلَانَةُ حَاضَتْ. وجه : (۱) الحديث لثبوت وَإِذَا اخْتَلَفَا فِي وُجُودِ الشَّرْطِ «الْبَيِّنَةُ عَلَى مَنِ ادَّعَى ، وَالْيَمِينُ عَلَى مَنْ أَنْكَرَ (سنن دار قطني: ١٤ - كتاب الحدود والديات وغيره،نمبر3190) وجه: (1)قول التابعى لثبوت فَإِنْ كَانَ الشَّرْطُ لَا يُعْلَمُ ا«عَنِ الشَّعْيِّ، قَالُوا: «تَجُوزُ شَهَادَةُ اصول: کلما کے علاوہ تمام الفاظ شرط سے ایک ہی بار قسم پوری ہو گی اور ایک ہی طلاق ہو گی۔ اصول: كُلَّما کا تقاضا ملک اول تک رہتا ہے، اس کے بعد ختم ہو جاتا ہے۔
مِثْلَ أَنْ يَقُولَ إِذَا حِضْت فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ فَقَالَتْ قَدْ حِضْت طَلُقَتْ وَإِذَا قَالَ إِنْ حِضْتَ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ وَفُلَانَةُ مَعَكَ فَقَالَتْ حِضْتَ طَلُقَتْ هِيَ وَلَمْ تَطْلُقْ فُلَانَةُ حَاضَتْ
And if they dispute about the existence of the condition, the word is the word of the husband unless the woman brings evidence. But if the condition is not known except from her side, then her word is accepted regarding herself. For example, if he says: 'When you menstruate, you are divorced,' and she says: 'I menstruated,' she is divorced. And if he says: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced, and so-and-so is with you,' and she says: 'I menstruated,' she is divorced but so-and-so is not divorced. Evidence: (1) The hadith for establishing when they dispute: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.' (Sunan Dar Qutni, no. 3190) Evidence: (1) The saying of the tābi'ī for establishing that if the condition is not known: from al-Sha‘bī, they said: 'The testimony of... [incomplete]' Principle: All condition words except kullamā fulfill the oath once and cause one divorce. Principle: The requirement of kullamā remains until the first ownership ends, after which it ceases.
Example: If he says: 'When you menstruate, you are divorced,' and she says: 'I menstruated,' she is divorced. And if he says: 'If you menstruate, you are divorced and so-and-so is with you,' and she says: 'I menstruated,' she is divorced but so-and-so is not divorced because she menstruated.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on disputes between husband and wife regarding the occurrence of a condition in conditional divorce. The default rule is that the husband's word is taken (i.e., if he denies the condition occurred, his statement is accepted) unless the wife can produce evidence (bayyinah) to the contrary. This is based on the general legal principle that the burden of proof lies on the claimant and the oath on the denier. However, an exception applies: if the condition is something only the wife can know (e.g., her own menstruation), then her word is accepted concerning herself. For example, if the husband says 'When you menstruate, you are divorced,' and she claims she menstruated, she is divorced based on her word. If the husband ties her divorce to another woman's menstruation (e.g., 'If you menstruate while so-and-so is with you'), and she claims she menstruated, only she is divorced; the other woman is not divorced because the condition for the other woman's divorce (her own menstruation) is not established solely by the wife's claim. The text also includes the Urdu principle: all non-kullamā condition words cause one divorce, and kullamā's effect lasts only during the first marriage.

Key Terms
الْبَيِّنَة (al-bayyinah): Evidence or proof, typically witness testimony in Hanafi fiqh.
الْيَمِين (al-yamīn): Oath taken by the defendant to deny the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'If you go to the market, you are divorced.' The wife claims she went, the husband denies. Who is believed?
A) Wife, because she knows her actions
B) Husband, because it is a public matter
C) Wife, if she brings one witness
D) Neither, requires investigation

Key Takeaway
In disputes over divorce conditions, the husband's word is default; the wife's word is accepted only on matters exclusively known to her, like menstruation.

Timing of Conditional Divorce Upon Menstruation
Ruling that divorce for condition 'when you menstruate' occurs after three consecutive days of blood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of conditional divorce tied to menstruation to determine when divorce takes effect, distinguishing between the two common phrasings.

Core Idea
Divorce conditional on menstruation requires three days of blood for 'idhā ḥiḍti' but full purity for 'in ḥiḍta ḥayḍatan'.

This passage deals with a common scenario in family law: a husband makes divorce conditional on his wife's menstruation. The wording of the condition determines precisely when the divorce takes effect. The Hanafi school relies on the definition of a valid menstruation (minimum three days) and the distinction between 'starting' a menstruation and 'completing' one. Understanding these nuances prevents premature rulings on marital status.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two phrasings
The text presents two conditional phrasings: 'idhā ḥiḍti' (when you menstruate) and 'in ḥiḍta ḥayḍatan' (if you menstruate one menstruation). Note that the second phrasing explicitly mentions a complete menstrual cycle.
The Arabic word 'ḥayḍatan' (one menstruation) is key.
2
Apply the minimum menstruation rule
For the first phrasing, divorce does not occur upon seeing blood but only after bleeding continues for three full days, because a valid menstruation in the Hanafi school must last at least three days. This is based on the statement of Khālid ibn Ma‘dān.
The three-day rule is a defining feature of Hanafi ḥayḍ.
3
Determine retroactive effect
Once three days are complete, the divorce is retroactively effective from the first moment she saw blood. This means that any actions during those three days are affected as if divorce occurred at the beginning.
This has implications for iddah and marital rights.
4
Differentiate the second phrasing
For 'in ḥiḍta ḥayḍatan', divorce is delayed until she completes the entire menstruation and performs ghusl (becomes pure). The proof from al-Thawrī explains that this phrasing requires fulfillment of a full cycle, not just the start of bleeding.
The wife cannot be taken back until she bathes, indicating the divorce occurs after ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا قَالَ لَهَا إِذَا حِضْتِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ فَرَأَتْ الدَّمَ لَم تَطْلُقْ حَتَّى يَسْتَمِرَّ ثَلَاثَةَ أَيَّامٍ فَإِذَا تَمَّتْ ثَلَاثَةُ أَيَّامٍ حَكَمْنَا بِالطَّلَاقِ مِنْ حِينِ رُؤْيَتِهَا الدَّمَ وَإِذَا قَالَ لَهَا إِنْ حِضْت حَيْضَةً فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ لَمْ تَطْلُقَ حَتَّى تَطْهُرَ مِنْ حَيْضَتِهَا. وجه : (۱)قول التابعى لثبوت وَإِذَا قَالَ لَهَا إِذَا حِضْتِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ سَمِعْتُ خَالِدَ بْنَ مَعْدَانَ قَالَ: «أَقَلُّ مَا تَكُونُ حَيْضَةُ الْمَرْأَةِ ثَلَاثَةَ أَيَّامٍ وَآخِرُهَا عَشَرَةٌ» (مصنف ابن شيبه: ما قالوا في الحيض؟ ، نمبر 19299) وجه (۱) قول التابعى لثبوت وَإِذَا قَالَ لَهَا إِنْ حِضْتَ حَيْضَةً فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ لَمْ تَطْلُقْ عَنِ الثَّوْرِيِّ، قَالَ فِي رَجُلٍ قَالَ لِامْرَأَتِهِ: إِذَا حِضْتِ حَيْضَةً فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ، أَوْ قَالَ: مَتَى حِضْتِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ قَالَ : " أَمَّا الَّتِي قَالَ: إِذَا حِضْتِ فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ، فَإِذَا دَخَلْتِ فِي الدَّمِ طُلِقْتِ، وَأَمَّا الَّتِي قَالَ: مَتَى حِصْتِ حَيْضَةً، فَحَتَّى تَغْتَسِلَ مِنْ آخِرِ حَيْضَتِهَا، لِأَنَّهَا لَا يُرَاجِعُهَا حَتَّى تَغْتَسِلَ " (مصنف عبدالرزاق باب الطلاق إلى أجل، نمبر 11321)
And if he says to her: 'When you menstruate, you are divorced,' and she sees blood, she is not divorced until it continues for three days. Then when three days are complete, we judge the divorce from the time she saw the blood. And if he says to her: 'If you menstruate one menstruation, you are divorced,' she is not divorced until she becomes pure from her menstruation. Evidence: (1) The saying of the tābi'ī for establishing: I heard Khālid ibn Ma‘dān say: 'The minimum of a woman's menstruation is three days and its maximum is ten.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 19299) Evidence: (1) The saying of the tābi'ī for establishing: from al-Thawrī about a man who said to his wife: 'If you menstruate one menstruation, you are divorced' or 'When you menstruate, you are divorced.' He said: 'As for the one who said "when you menstruate," when she enters the blood, she is divorced. As for the one who said "when you menstruate one menstruation," then until she bathes from the end of her menstruation, because she cannot be taken back until she bathes.' (Musannaf ‘Abd al-Razzāq, no. 11321)
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage provides two related rulings on conditional divorce tied to menstruation, with proofs from early authorities. The first scenario: a husband says 'When you menstruate (idhā ḥiḍti), you are divorced.' If the wife sees blood, she is not instantly divorced. Divorce occurs only after the bleeding continues for a full three days (the minimum duration of a valid menstruation in the Hanafi school). Once three days have passed, the divorce is retroactively effective from the moment she first saw blood. This is based on the statement of the tābi‘ī Khālid ibn Ma‘dān that the minimum menstruation is three days. The second scenario: the husband uses the phrase 'If you menstruate one menstruation (in ḥiḍta ḥayḍatan).' Here, divorce is delayed until she completes her menstruation and becomes pure (i.e., the bleeding stops and she performs ghusl). The proof from al-Thawrī differentiates between the two phrasings: with 'idhā ḥiḍti' divorce occurs upon starting the blood (but conditioned on three days), whereas with 'in ḥiḍta ḥayḍatan' divorce occurs only after full completion. The underlying reason is that the latter phrasing explicitly ties the condition to a complete menstrual cycle, which ends with purification.

Key Terms
الحيض (al-ḥayḍ): Menstruation; in this context, the condition for divorce.
الطهر (al-ṭuhr): Purity from menstruation (cessation of bleeding and completion of ghusl).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says: 'When you menstruate, you are divorced.' The wife sees blood and it continues for three consecutive days. When does the divorce take effect according to the Hanafi school?
A) At the moment she saw the blood, retroactively
B) After the third day ends
C) After she performs ghusl
D) Only if she bathes after the third day

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires three days of continuous bleeding for the condition 'idhā ḥiḍti' to trigger divorce, while 'in ḥiḍta ḥayḍatan' requires completion of the full menstrual cycle including ghusl.

Divorce of a Slave Woman: Two Divorces Only
Ruling with proof on slave woman's divorce count

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of divorces for a slave woman in Hanafi fiqh and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A slave woman can only be divorced twice, regardless of the husband's status.

In this lesson, we examine a clear ruling from the Hanafi school on the number of divorces applicable to a slave woman. The ruling is derived from a specific hadith and grounded in the principle that a slave's legal capacity is half that of a free person. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying divorce rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for slave woman's divorce
The passage states that the divorce of a slave woman is two divorces, whether her husband is free or a slave. This is the core ruling.
The ruling is unconditional regarding the husband's status.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The proof is a hadith from Aisha (RA) that the Prophet (PBUH) said: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces, and her waiting period is two menstruations.' This hadith is found in Sunan Abi Dawud and Tirmidhi.
The hadith directly specifies the number.
3
Understand the rationale
The Urdu principles explain that a slave woman's status (ni'mah) is half that of a free woman in many legal matters. Since a free woman's maximum divorces are three, half would be one and a half, but divorce is indivisible, so it becomes two.
This explains why two, not one and a half.
4
Note the cross-madhhab agreement
The Shafi'i school also holds that a slave woman's divorce is two. This is accepted by the majority of scholars, based on the same hadith.
Minimal ikhtilaf exists among early scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَطَلَاقُ الْأَمَةِ تَطْلِيقَتَانِ حُرًّا كَانَ زَوْجُهَا أَوْ عَبْدًا. وجه: (ا) الحديث لثبوت وَطَلَاقُ الْأَمَةِ تَطْلِيقَتَانِ حُرًّا اعَنْ عَائِشَةَ عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «طَلَاقُ الْأَمَةِ تَطْلِيقَتَانِ، وَقُرُوؤُهَا حَيْضَتَانِ.» (ابوداؤد شریف باب في سنة طلاق العبد، نمبر 2189 ترمذي شريف : باب ما جاء أن طلاق الأمة تطليقتان،نمبر 1182)
اصول: باندی کی نعمت آزاد عورت کے مقابل آدھی ہے لہذا باندی کی مکمل دو ہی طلاق ہوں گی۔
اصول: طلاق منجزی نہیں ہوتی اسلئے تین طلاق کی آدھی ڈیڑھ کے بجائے دو ہوں گی۔
And the divorce of a slave woman is two divorces, whether her husband is free or a slave. Evidence: (1) The hadith for establishing that the divorce of a slave woman is two divorces: from 'Ā'ishah from the Prophet (PBUH) who said: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two divorces, and her waiting period is two menstruations.' (Abu Dāwūd, chapter on Sunnah of slave divorce, no. 2189; Tirmidhī, no. 1182) Principle: The blessing of a slave woman is half that of a free woman, therefore the complete (divorce) for a slave woman is two. Principle: Divorce is not divisible; therefore half of three divorces is not one and a half but two.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the number of divorces that can be pronounced against a slave woman in the Hanafi madhhab. The text states that a slave woman can only be divorced twice (tahlīqatān) to make her irrevocably separate from her husband, regardless of whether her husband is free or also a slave. This is based on the hadith narrated by 'Ā'ishah (RA) that the Prophet (PBUH) said the divorce of a slave woman is two and her waiting period is two menstruations. The Urdu principles explain the rationale: a slave woman's status (ni'mah) is half that of a free woman in many legal matters (e.g., diyah, hudud). Therefore, the maximum number of divorces, which for a free woman is three, is halved to two for a slave woman. Since divorce is not fractional, half of three is taken as two (not one and a half). Thus, after two divorces, the slave woman becomes completely separated (baynūnā kubrā), just as a free woman does after three.

Key Terms
الأمة (al-amah): A female slave; her legal capacity is half that of a free woman in many rulings.
الطلاق (al-ṭalāq): Divorce; the termination of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Third divorce is valid, she becomes completely separated
B) Third divorce is invalid, she is already separated after two
C) Only two divorces count; third is ignored
D) All three count if he is free

Key Takeaway
A slave woman's divorce is two, based on the hadith, and the third pronouncement is invalid.

Types of Delegation in Divorce: Tamlik and Tawkeel
Rulings on delegation of divorce (tamlik and tawkeel).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and tawkeel, apply the rule that tamlik is confined to the session unless made absolute, and determine the number and revocability of divorces in various delegation scenarios.

Core Idea
In tamlik, the wife may divorce herself only in the session; in tawkeel, the agent may divorce even after the session unless restricted.

This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on delegating the right to divorce. The source text distinguishes two forms: tamlik, where the husband makes his wife owner of the divorce right, and tawkeel, where he appoints an agent. Understanding the conditions of each is crucial because the consequences differ dramatically — particularly regarding the time limit and the ability to revoke.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of delegation
Read the text and note the two distinct scenarios: when the husband addresses the wife directly (tamlik) and when he appoints a third party (tawkeel).
Tamlik involves transferring the right; tawkeel is appointing an agent.
2
Analyze tamlik: session limit and exceptions
For tamlik, observe that the wife must act within the same session unless the husband uses wording like 'whenever you wish' (mutlaq). This is a key condition.
The default is session-bound; absolute wording expands the time.
3
Determine the number and revocability of divorces in tamlik
The text states that the default divorce is one revocable (raj‘i). If the wife pronounces three and the husband intended three, all three occur. Understand the role of intention.
Intent of the husband can affect the count.
4
Analyze tawkeel: unrestricted vs restricted agency
For tawkeel, if the husband says 'Divorce my wife,' the agent can act after the session and even revoke. If he says 'if you wish,' the agency is limited to the session.
The phrasing 'if you wish' restricts to the session.
5
Examine conditional divorce based on love or hate
When the husband conditions divorce on the wife's statement about love/hate, her outward expression triggers the divorce regardless of inner feelings. This relies on the principle of relying on outward expressions.
Inward contrary intent does not affect validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَا بُدَّ مِنْ ذِكْرِ النَّفْسِ فِي كَلَامِهِ أَوْ كَلَامِهَا مَا دَامَتْ فِي مَجْلِسِهَا فَإِنْ طَلَّقَتْ نَفْسَهَا فِي قَوْلِهِ طَلِقِي نَفْسَكَ فَهِيَ وَاحِدَةً رَجْعِيَّةٌ فَإِنْ طَلَّقَتْ نَفْسَهَا ثَلَاثًا وَقَدْ أَرَادَ الزَّوْجُ ذَلِكَ وَقَعْنَ عَلَيْهَا وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهَا طَلِّقِي نَفْسَكَ مَتَى شِئْتِ فَلَهَا أَنْ تُطَلَّقَ نَفْسَهَا فِي الْمَجْلِسِ وَبَعْدَهُ وَلَهُ أَنْ يَرْجِعَ وَإِنْ قَالَ طَلَّقْهَا إِنْ شِئْتِ فَلَهُ أَنْ يُطَلَّقَهَا فِي الْمَجْلِسِ خَاصَّةً وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهَا إِنْ كُنْتَ تُحِبِّينِي أَوْ تَبْغُضِينِي فَأَنْتِ طَالِقٌ فَقَالَتْ أَنَا أُحِبُّكَ أَوْ أَبْغَضُكَ وَقَعَ الطَّلَاقُ وَإِنْ كَانَ فِي قَلْبِهَا بِخِلَافِ مَا أَظْهَرَتْ
It is necessary to mention the self in his speech or her speech as long as she is in her session. If she divorces herself in his saying 'Divorce yourself,' then it is one revocable divorce. And if she divorces herself three times and the husband intended that, they occur upon her. And if he said to her 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish,' then she may divorce herself in the session and after it. And if he said to a man 'Divorce my wife,' then he may divorce her in the session and after it, and he may revoke. And if he said 'Divorce her if you wish,' then he may divorce her only in the session. And if he said to her 'If you love me or hate me, then you are divorced,' and she said 'I love you' or 'I hate you,' the divorce occurs even if in her heart it is contrary to what she displayed.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings on delegation of divorce (tafwid). It distinguishes between tamlik (making the wife owner of the divorce) and tawkeel (appointing an agent). The first ruling states that for tamlik, the wife must mention herself in her response within the same session; the divorce is revocable (raj‘i). If she pronounces three divorces and the husband intended that, all three occur. If the husband says 'whenever you wish,' the delegation becomes absolute and is not limited to the session. For agency (tawkeel) to a third man, the agent may divorce the wife in the session and after, and may also revoke. But if the husband says 'if you wish,' the agency is limited to the session. Finally, if the husband conditions divorce on her love or hatred, and she affirms either, divorce occurs regardless of her inner feelings, because the ruling is based on outward expression.

Key Terms
تمليك (tamlik): Making the wife owner of the right to divorce herself
توكيل (tawkeel): Appointing an agent to pronounce divorce

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'Divorce yourself whenever you wish,' and she divorces herself after the session, how many divorces occur?
A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three divorces
D) No divorce occurs

Key Takeaway
Tamlik binds the wife to the session unless made absolute; tawkeel allows the agent to act beyond the session unless restricted.

Divorce During Fatal Illness and Inheritance: The Hanafi Position
Ruling with proof on inheritance after ba'in divorce in fatal illness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on inheritance for a wife divorced irrevocably during marad al-mawt and identify the condition that triggers or blocks inheritance.

Core Idea
A wife divorced irrevocably during fatal illness still inherits if she is in ‘iddah at death – the divorce cannot defeat a rightful share.

This lesson examines a specific case of inheritance: what happens when a man divorces his wife irrevocably during a terminal illness and then dies. The Hanafi school, relying on the practice of Caliph Uthman, protects the wife’s inheritance rights while she remains in her waiting period. Understanding this ruling clarifies the interaction between divorce and inheritance, and reveals a key principle: a husband cannot use divorce to disinherit his wife on his deathbed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The husband divorces his wife during his fatal illness (marad al-mawt) with an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce.
2
Note the timing condition
The wife must still be in her ‘iddah when the husband dies. If the ‘iddah has expired, she does not inherit.
3
Apply the ruling
If death occurs during ‘iddah, she inherits; otherwise, she does not.
4
Identify the proof
The ruling is based on the practice of Caliph Uthman (ra), who enforced inheritance for a woman divorced irrevocably by Abd al-Rahman ibn Awf while she was in ‘iddah.
5
Recognize the dispute
The Shafi'i school holds that a ba'in divorce severs all rights, including inheritance, even if the death occurs during ‘iddah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الرَّجُلُ امْرَأَتَهُ فِي مَرَضِ مَوْتِهِ طَلَاقًا بَائِنًا فَمَاتَ وَهِيَ فِي الْعِدَّةِ وَرِثَتْ مِنْهُ وَإِنْ مَاتَ بَعْدَ انْقِضَاءِ عِدَّتِهَا فَلَا مِيرَاثَ لَهَا
And if a man divorces his wife during his fatal illness with an irrevocable divorce, then he dies while she is in her ‘iddah, she inherits from him. And if he dies after the expiry of her ‘iddah, then she has no inheritance.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on divorce during marad al-mawt (fatal illness). If the husband pronounces a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce while terminally ill, the wife still inherits from him if she is in her ‘iddah at the time of his death. This is to prevent the husband from depriving his wife of inheritance by divorcing her when death is imminent. However, if the ‘iddah expires before his death, the marriage tie is completely severed and she does not inherit. The text also implicitly contrasts with the Shafi'i view, where some companions (Ibn al-Zubayr) held that a mabtutah (irrevocably divorced) woman does not inherit. The Hanafi reliance on the practice of Uthman (ra) and the ruling of Shuraih supports the inheritance during ‘iddah.

Key Terms
الطلاق البائن (al-talaq al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately without right of return
عدة ('iddah): Waiting period after divorce during which the wife cannot remarry

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Yes, because the divorce was during illness
B) No, because the ‘iddah expired before his death
C) Yes, she inherits half
D) No, a ba'in divorce always nullifies inheritance

Key Takeaway
A wife divorced irrevocably during fatal illness inherits from her husband if she is still in her ‘iddah at his death; this protects her from being disinherited by a deathbed divorce.

Ruling and Methods of Raj'ah (Revocation)
States ruling of raj'ah and defines methods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling of raj'ah, identify its methods, and explain why the wife's consent is not required.

Core Idea
Raj'ah is a unilateral right of the husband during the iddah, effected by word or physical acts of desire.

This lesson introduces the Book of Revocation (Kitab al-Raj'ah) from Al-Quduri's matn. After a revocable divorce, the husband has the right to take his wife back during her waiting period without her consent. The passage defines what constitutes a valid revocation and emphasizes that witnessing is recommended but not required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for raj'ah
Start by noting that raj'ah applies only after a revocable divorce (one or two) and only during the iddah (waiting period). These are the two essential conditions.
The iddah must not have expired.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the husband may revoke his wife unilaterally; her consent is irrelevant. This is a right (mubah), not an obligation.
3
List the methods of raj'ah
The passage lists explicit verbal declaration (using 'rāja'tuk') and physical acts done with sexual desire: intercourse, kissing, touching, or looking at the private parts with desire.
Physical acts without desire do not effect raj'ah.
4
Note the recommendation of witnesses
The author states it is mustahab (recommended) to have two witnesses, but the absence of witnesses does not invalidate the revocation.
This distinguishes Hanafi view from some other schools.
5
Summarize the legal consequence
If any valid method is performed, the raj'ah is effective, and the marriage continues as before without a new contract.
The iddah ends immediately upon revocation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الرَّجْعَةِ
قَالَ - رَحِمَهُ اللهُ - وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الرَّجُلُ امْرَأَتَهُ تَطْلِيقَةً رَجْعِيَّةً أَوْ تَطْلِيقَتَيْنِ فَلَهُ أَنْ يُرَاجِعَهَا فِي عِدَّتِهَا رَضِيَتْ بِذَلِكَ أَوْ لَمْ تَرْضَ
وَالرَّجْعَةُ أَنْ يَقُولَ رَاجَعْتُك أَوْ رَاجَعْتُ امْرَأَتِي أَوْ يَطَأَهَا أَوْ يُقَبِلَهَا أَوْ يَلْمِسَهَا بِشَهْوَةٍ أَوْ يَنْظُرَ إِلَى فَرْجِهَا بِشَهْوَةِ
وَيُسْتَحَبُّ أَنْ يُشْهِدَ عَلَى الرَّجْعَةِ شَاهِدَيْنِ وَإِذَا لَمْ يُشْهِدْ صَحَّتْ الرَّجْعَةُ
اصول: شوہر ایک یا دو طلاق رجعی کے بعد عدت کے اندر رجعت کر سکتا ہے ، خواہ عورت راضی ہو یا نہ ہو۔
Book of Revocation. He (the author) said - may Allah have mercy on him -: When a man divorces his wife with one revocable divorce or two, he may revoke her during her waiting period, whether she agrees or not. And revocation is that he says 'I have revoked you' or 'I have revoked my wife', or he has intercourse with her, or kisses her, or touches her with desire, or looks at her private part with desire. And it is recommended that he have two witnesses to the revocation, but if he does not have witnesses, the revocation is valid. Principle: A husband can revoke after one or two revocable divorces during the iddah, whether the woman is pleased or not.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from the matn of Al-Quduri. It opens the Book of Revocation (Kitab al-Raj'ah). The passage states the basic rule: after a revocable divorce (whether one or two revocations), the husband has the right to take his wife back during her 'iddah (waiting period). This right is unilateral; it does not require the wife's consent. Then it defines the methods of revocation: either by explicit verbal declaration using the root r-j-', or by certain physical acts that imply desire, such as intercourse, kissing, touching with desire, or looking at the private parts with desire. These physical acts, when done with sexual desire, effect a revocation even without words. The passage then mentions that it is recommended (mustahab) to have two witnesses to the revocation, but the absence of witnesses does not invalidate it; the revocation remains valid. The Urdu note summarizes: a husband can revoke after one or two revocable divorces during the iddah, whether the woman likes it or not.

Key Terms
الرَّجْعَةُ (al-raj'ah): The act of a husband taking back his wife after a revocable divorce during the iddah, by word or physical intimacy with desire.
تَطْلِيقَةً رَجْعِيَّةً (tatliqatan raj'iyyatan): A divorce that is revocable, i.e., the first or second divorce where the husband can take the wife back without a new marriage contract.
الْعِدَّةُ (al-'iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death before she can remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following acts does NOT constitute a valid raj'ah?
A) Saying 'I have revoked you'
B) Intercourse with desire
C) Looking at her face without desire
D) Kissing with desire

Key Takeaway
Raj'ah is a unilateral right of the husband, effected by word or physical acts of desire during the iddah, and does not require witnesses or the wife's consent.

Textual Proofs for Revocable Divorce (Raj'ah)
Citing multiple textual proofs for raj'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four primary textual proofs for the permissibility of raj'ah and explain why the Hanafi school holds that witnesses are recommended but not obligatory.

Core Idea
Multiple verses and hadith prove raj'ah; witnesses are recommended, not required.

This lesson unpacks the evidentiary foundation for the rulings on revocable divorce (raj'ah). The author cites four wujuh from the Quran and Sunnah, and then adds statements of Companions and Successors to support the recommendation of having witnesses. Understanding these proofs is essential to grasp why the Hanafi school considers raj'ah valid without witnesses, unlike some other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling being proven
The passage proves that after a first or second revocable divorce, the husband may take back his wife during the iddah without a new marriage contract. Also, it proves that having witnesses is recommended (mandub) but not obligatory.
The ruling is already stated in the earlier matn; here the author provides the proofs.
2
Extract the primary proofs (wujuh 1-4)
List the four main evidences: (1) Quran 2:229 – 'imsaakun bi ma'ruf'; (2) Mujahid's tafsir of Quran 2:228 – 'husbands are more entitled during that [iddah]'; (3) Hadith of Ibn Umar – the Prophet ordered him to take her back; (4) Quran 65:2 – 'either keep them in an acceptable manner and call to witness two just men'. Identify the operative phrases.
Wajh (1) and (2) support the permissibility; wajh (4) relates to witnesses.
3
Analyze the Hanafi interpretation of the witness verse
Quran 65:2 uses the imperative 'wa ashhidu' (call to witness). Hanafi usul distinguishes between an obligation (wujub) and a recommendation (nadb). Here, the Prophet's practice and the statements of Companions show that witnesses are not mandatory; thus the command is interpreted as recommendation.
This is the key point of ikhtilaf with the Shafi'i school.
4
Examine the additional athar for witness recommendation
The passage cites statements from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i, Abdullah ibn Mas'ud, and Imran ibn Husayn. These show that early authorities considered witnessing good practice but not a condition for validity. This supports the Hanafi position.
These are labeled as 'face' (a) and (2) in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) الآية لثبوت وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الرَّجُلُ امْرَأَتَهُ تَطْلِيقَةً رَجْعِيَّةً \ الطَّلَقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكُ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحُ بِإِحْسَن (القرآن سورة البقرة2، الآية، نمبر 229) وجه : (۱) وَهِيَ حَائِضَ، فَذَكَرَ عُمَرُ لِلنَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم فَقَالَ : لِيُرَاجِعْهَا(بخاري شريف: باب إذا طلقت الحائض تعتد بذلك الطلاق، نمبر 5252) وجه (۳) الآية لثبوت وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الرَّجُلُ نمبر 2) وجه : (۴) قول التابعى لثبوت وَإِذَا طَلَّقَ الرَّجُلُ امْرَأَتَهُ تَطْلِيقَةً رَجْعِيَّةً \ عَنْ إِبْرَاهِيمَ قَالَ: «إِذَا ادَّعَى الرَّجْعَةَ قَبْلَ انْقِضَاءِ الْعِدَّةِ، فَعَلَيْهِ الْبَيِّنَةُ» (مصنف ابن شيبه: ما قالوا في الرجل يدعي الرجعة قبل انقضاء العدة، نمبر 19212) وجه: (ا) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَيُسْتَحَبُّ أَنْ يُشْهِدَ عَلَى الرَّجْعَةِ شَاهِدَيْنِ \ عَنْ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ قَالَ: "إِذَا طَلَّقَ سِرًّا رَاجَعَ سِرَّاذَلِكَ رَجْعَةٌ، فَإِنْ وَاقَعَ فَلَا بَأْسَ، وَإِنْ طَلَّقَ عَلَى نِيَّتِهِ وَرَاجَعَ، فَلْيُشْهِدْ عَلَى رَجْعَتِهِ، (مصنف ابن شيبه ما قالوا إذا طلق سرا راجع سرا، نمبر 19225) وجه (۲) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَيُسْتَحَبُّ أَنْ يُشْهِدَ عَلَى الرَّجْعَةِ شَاهِدَيْنِ \ «أَنَّ عِمْرَانَ بْنَ حُصَيْنٍ سُئِلَ عَنِ الرَّجُلِ يُطَلِّقُ امْرَأَتَهُ، ثُمَّ يَقَعُ بِهَا، وَلَمْ يُشْهِدْ عَلَى طَلَاقِهَا، وَلَا عَلَى رَجْعَتِهَا فَقَالَ: طَلَّقْتَ لِغَيْر سُنَّةٍ، وَرَاجَعْتَ لِغَيْرِ سُنَّةِ أَشْهِدْ عَلَى طَلَاقِهَا، وَعَلَى رَجْعَتِهَا، وَلَا تَعُدْ.» (ابوداؤد شريف: باب الرجل يراجع ولا يشهد نمبر، 2186 ابن ماجه : باب الرجعة ،نمبر 2025)
(1) The verse for establishing: And when a man divorces his wife with a revocable divorce: 'Divorce is twice. Then either keep [her] in an acceptable manner or release [her] with good treatment.' (Quran 2:229) Evidence (1): Statement of a Successor for establishing: And when a man divorces his wife with a revocable divorce: From Mujahid, regarding His statement: 'And their husbands are more entitled to take them back during that [period].' (Quran 2:228) He said: 'Meaning during the waiting period.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Book of Raj'ah, no. 15149) Evidence (2): Hadith for establishing: And when a man divorces his wife with a revocable divorce: I heard Ibn Umar say: 'Ibn Umar divorced his wife while she was menstruating. Umar mentioned that to the Prophet, peace be upon him, and he said: Let him take her back.' (Sahih al-Bukhari: Chapter on if a menstruating woman is divorced, she observes her iddah by that divorce, no. 5252) Evidence (3): The verse for establishing: And when a man divorces his wife with a revocable divorce: 'So when they have [almost] reached their term, either keep them in an acceptable manner or separate from them in an acceptable manner. And call to witness two just men among you.' (Quran 65:2) Evidence (4): Statement of a Successor for establishing: And when a man divorces his wife with a revocable divorce: From Ibrahim who said: 'When he claims the raj'ah before the expiration of the iddah, he must produce evidence.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah: What they said about a man who claims raj'ah before expiration of iddah, no. 19212) Evidence (a): Statement of a Companion for establishing: And it is recommended to have two witnesses to the raj'ah: From Abdullah who said: 'If he divorces secretly, he may revoke secretly: that is a revocation. If he has intercourse, there is no harm. But if he divorces with his intention and then revokes, let him have witnesses to his revocation.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah: What they said about a man who divorces secretly and revokes secretly, no. 19225) Evidence (2): Statement of a Companion for establishing: And it is recommended to have two witnesses to the raj'ah: That Imran ibn Husayn was asked about a man who divorces his wife, then has intercourse with her, without having witnesses to the divorce or the raj'ah. He said: 'You have divorced contrary to the sunnah and revoked contrary to the sunnah. Have witnesses to the divorce and the raj'ah, and do not do it again.' (Abu Dawud: Chapter on a man who revokes without witnesses, no. 2186; Ibn Majah: Chapter on raj'ah, no. 2025)
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage provides the evidentiary basis for the rulings on raj'ah stated in the previous matn. It is structured as a series of wujuh (proofs) from the text's commentary. Each proof is labeled (1), (2), etc. The first proof is from Quran 2:229: 'Divorce is twice. Then either keep [her] in an acceptable manner or release [her] with good treatment.' This verse shows that after one or two divorces, the husband may keep the wife (by raj'ah) or let her go. The second proof is the interpretation of Quran 2:228 by the Successor Mujahid, who says 'and their husbands are more entitled to take them back during that period' refers to the waiting period. The third proof is the hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet commanded him to take his wife back after divorcing her during menstruation, establishing that raj'ah is permissible even during menses. The fourth proof is from Quran 65:2, which commands taking wives back in an acceptable manner and having witnesses. The fifth proof is a statement of the Successor Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that if a man claims raj'ah before iddah ends, he must bring evidence. This implies that the claim is acceptable in principle. Finally, two statements from the Companion Abdullah ibn Mas'ud and the Companion Imran ibn Husayn support the recommendation of having witnesses for the raj'ah, though it is not mandatory. The passage does not explicitly say which proofs are for which part, but the context shows they support the rulings of the matn.

Key Terms
وجہ (wajh): A proof or evidence cited in support of a ruling; here used to list different textual evidences.
قول التابعي (qawl al-tabi'i): The statement of a Successor (a scholar from the generation after the Companions) used as supporting evidence.
قول الصحابي (qawl al-sahabi): The statement of a Companion of the Prophet used as evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is cited as evidence for the recommendation (not obligation) of having witnesses for raj'ah?
A) Quran 2:229
B) Hadith of Ibn Umar
C) Statement of Abdullah ibn Mas'ud
D) Statement of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school derives the permissibility of raj'ah from multiple texts, and holds that witnesses are recommended but not obligatory, in contrast to some other schools that require witnesses.

Claim of Raj'ah After Iddah: Three Scenarios
Three cases of raj'ah claim after iddah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings for three scenarios where a husband claims raj'ah after the iddah has ended, and identify which party's word is taken according to Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
When the wife denies the husband's claim of raj'ah after iddah, the husband's word is taken without an oath—but if she asserts that her iddah has ended, her word prevails.

This passage from Al-Quduri presents three distinct cases regarding claims of revocation (raj'ah) after the waiting period (iddah) has expired. Each case turns on who is believed when there is a dispute. Understanding these rulings requires careful parsing of the conditions and the identity of the parties involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three cases described: (1) husband claims raj'ah, wife confirms; (2) husband claims raj'ah, wife denies; (3) wife replies that iddah ended. Also the slave woman case: husband claims, master confirms, slave woman denies.
2
Determine who is claiming and who is denying
For each scenario, identify the claimant and the denier. The ruling changes based on whether the wife or husband is the one disputing the fact.
In scenario 3, the wife is denying that the revocation occurred during iddah.
3
Apply the rule of al-qawl qawlu man
The Hanafi principle is that in disputes over a claim, one party's statement is given precedence. State whose word is taken in each case.
In the first denial case, husband's word is taken without oath. In the wife's iddah-ended claim, her word is taken. In the slave woman case, her word is taken over the master's.
4
Note the exception regarding oath
According to Abu Hanifa, no oath is required from the husband when his word is taken. This differs from other schools.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا انْقَضَتْ الْعِدَّةُ فَقَالَ قَدْ كُنْت رَاجَعْتهَا فِي الْعِدَّةِ فَصَدَّقَتْهُ فَهِيَ رَجْعَةٌ وَإِنْ كَذَّبَتْهُ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْهُنَا وَلَا يَمِينَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ ه وَإِذَا قَالَ الزَّوْجُ قَدْ رَاجَعْتُكَ فَقَالَتْ مُحِيبَةً لَهُ قَدْ انْقَضَتْ عِدَّتِي لَمْ تَصِحَ الرَّجْعَةُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَإِذَا قَالَ زَوْجُ الْأَمَةِ بَعْدَ انْقِضَاءِ عِدَّتِهَا قَدْ كُنْت رَاجَعْتهَا فَصَدَّقَهُ الْمَوْلَى وَكَذَّبَتْهُ الْأَمَةُ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْها لا
وجه (۱) دليل امام محمد الحديث لثبوت وَإِذَا انْقَطَعَ الدَّمُ مِنْ الْخَيْضَةِ الثَّالِثَةِ \ عَنْ أَبِي أُمَامَةَ الْبَاهِلِي ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لَا يَكُونُ الْخَيْضُ لِلْجَارِيَةِ وَالثَّيِّبِ الَّتِي قَدْ أَيسَتْ مِنَ الخيض أقل مِنْ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ وَلا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ عَشَرَةِ أَيَّامٍ ، فَإِذَا رَأتِ الدَّمَ فَوْقَ عَشَرَةِ أَيَّامٍ فَهِيَ مُسْتَحَاضَةٌ (سنن دار قطني : ۲ – كتاب الحيض،نمبر 845)
And when the waiting period ends, and he says, 'I had revoked her during the waiting period,' and she confirms him, then it is a revocation. But if she denies him, then the say is his word, and there is no oath according to Abu Hanifa. And when the husband says, 'I have revoked you,' and she says, responding to him, 'My waiting period has ended,' the revocation is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. And when the husband of a slave woman says after the expiration of her waiting period, 'I had revoked her,' and the master confirms him but the slave woman denies him, then the say is her word.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents three specific cases involving claims of raj'ah after the iddah has ended. First: If the iddah has expired and the husband claims he revoked during the iddah, the ruling depends on the wife's confirmation. If she confirms, the revocation is valid. If she denies, the husband's word is taken (i.e., he is believed without needing to swear an oath) according to Abu Hanifa. This is based on the principle that the husband's claim of a past event is accepted when the other party disputes it, because he is claiming a right for himself. Second: If the husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife responds 'My iddah has ended' (meaning she denies that he revoked within the iddah), the revocation is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. This is because she is denying that a valid revocation took place during the iddah, and her denial is accepted since the iddah's end is a factual matter that she can know. Third: For a slave woman married to a free man, the husband's claim of revocation after her iddah is accepted if the master confirms, but if the slave woman denies, her word is taken. This is because the slave woman's testimony regarding her own iddah (which involves her menstruation) is considered more reliable. The proof at the bottom of the page (from Abu Umamah) actually relates to the next lesson about the duration of menses and when the iddah ends, not to these rulings. It appears misplaced in the OCR.

Key Terms
القول قوله (al-qawl qawlahu): The legal maxim that in a dispute, one party's statement is given precedence; here, the husband's word is taken when claiming raj'ah after iddah if the wife denies.
لا يمين (la yamin): No oath required; the husband is believed without swearing.
عدة (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the iddah has ended and the husband claims he revoked during the iddah, but the wife denies, what is the ruling according to Abu Hanifa?
A) The revocation is invalid because the wife denies
B) The husband's word is taken without oath
C) The husband must swear an oath
D) The wife's word is taken

Key Takeaway
In disputes over raj'ah after iddah, the Hanafi school distinguishes between the wife's denial of the claim (husband believed) and her assertion that iddah ended (wife believed).

End of Iddah when Third Menses Stops: Ghusl, Tayammum, or Prayer?
Ruling on when the iddah ends based on cessation of blood from the third menses, with intra-Hanafi difference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa/Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani on when the revocation period ends after the third menses, and identify the evidence for each.

Core Idea
The iddah does not end automatically when bleeding stops; the husband's right to revoke continues until ghusl or tayammum with prayer.

The exact moment when a divorced woman's iddah ends is critical because it determines the husband's right to revoke the divorce. The Hanafi school agrees that the third menses completes the iddah, but the question is: does the revocation period end immediately when bleeding stops, or only after she performs purification? This lesson analyzes the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa & Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the other, examining their proofs and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common ground
All Hanafi imams agree that the iddah is completed by the third menses and that if the blood stops at ten days (the maximum), the iddah ends immediately without ghusl.
The text states: 'When the blood stops from the third menses at ten days, the waiting period ends, even if she does not perform ghusl.'
2
State the point of disagreement
When the blood stops before ten days, what marks the end of the revocation period? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf require ghusl or tayammum with prayer; Muhammad considers tayammum alone sufficient.
The phrase 'according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf' and 'Muhammad said' introduces the ikhtilaf.
3
Examine the evidence for the majority view
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rely on the statement of Umar and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: 'He has more right to her until she performs ghusl from the third menses.' This indicates that ghusl is the marker.
The source quotes the athar from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah.
4
Examine the evidence for Muhammad's view
Muhammad uses the hadith of Abu Umamah that sets the maximum menstruation at ten days. He deduces that when bleeding stops, the woman is in a state of purity, and tayammum (a substitute for ghusl) is enough to end the revocation period.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Dar Qutni; note the wording 'الخيض' for 'الحيض' is a scribal variant.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If a woman's third menses stops at less than ten days and she performs tayammum but does not pray, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf the husband can still revoke; according to Muhammad he cannot.
This scenario is tested in the checkpoint.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا انْقَطَعَ الدَّمُ مِنْ الخَيْضَةِ الثَّالِثَةِ لِعَشْرَةِ أَيَّامٍ انْقَضَتْ الْعِدَّةُ وَإِنْ لَمْ تَغْتَسِلُ وَإِنْ انْقَطَعَ لِأَقَلَّ مِنْ عَشْرَةِ أَيَّامٍ لَمْ تَنْقَطِعْ الرَّجْعَةُ حَتَّى تَغْتَسِلَ أَوْ يَمْضِيَ عَلَيْهَا وَقْتُ الصَّلَاةِ أَوْ تَتَيَمَّمُ وَتُصَلِّي عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ إِذَا تَيَمَّمَتْ انْقَطَعَتْ الرَّجْعَةُ وَإِنْ لَمْ تُصَلَّ
وجه (۱) قول الصحابي لثبوت وَإِذَا انْقَطَعَ الدَّمُ مِنْ الْخَيْضَةِ الثَّالِثَةِ \ عَنْ عُمَرَ، وَعَبْدِ اللَّهِ قَالَا: «هُوَ أَحَقُّ بِمَا حَتَّى تَغْتَسِلَ مِنَ الْخَيْضَةِ الثَّالِثَةِ» (مصنف ابن شيبه: من قال: هو أحق برجعتها ما لم تغتسل من الحيضة الثالثة، نمبر 18898)
وجه (۱) دليل امام محمد الحديث لثبوت وَإِذَا انْقَطَعَ الدَّمُ مِنْ الْخَيْضَةِ الثَّالِثَةِ \ عَنْ أَبِي أُمَامَةَ الْبَاهِلِي ، قَالَ: قَالَ رَسُولُ اللهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم: «لَا يَكُونُ الْخَيْضُ لِلْجَارِيَةِ وَالثَّيِّبِ الَّتِي قَدْ أَيسَتْ مِنَ الخيض أقل مِنْ ثَلَاثَةِ أَيَّامٍ وَلا أَكْثَرَ مِنْ عَشَرَةِ أَيَّامٍ ، فَإِذَا رَأتِ الدَّمَ فَوْقَ عَشَرَةِ أَيَّامٍ فَهِيَ مُسْتَحَاضَةٌ (سنن دار قطني : ۲ – كتاب الحيض،نمبر 845)
اصول: تیسرے حیض کا دس دن میں خون بند ہوا تو اب رجعت کا وقت ختم ہو گیا اور عدت پوری ہو گئی۔
And when the blood stops from the third menses at ten days, the waiting period ends, even if she does not perform ghusl. And if it stops at less than ten days, the revocation does not cease until she performs ghusl, or the time for prayer passes, or she performs tayammum and prays, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. And Muhammad said: When she performs tayammum, the revocation ceases even if she does not pray. Evidence (1): Statement of a Companion for establishing: And when the blood stops from the third menses: From Umar and Abdullah, they said: 'He has more right to her until she performs ghusl from the third menses.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah: Who said he has more right to revoke her as long as she has not performed ghusl from the third menses, no. 18898) Evidence (1): Proof of Imam Muhammad for establishing: And when the blood stops from the third menses: From Abu Umamah al-Bahili, he said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, said: 'Menstruation for a young girl and a woman who has despaired of menstruation is not less than three days nor more than ten days. So when she sees blood beyond ten days, she is mustahadah.' (Sunan al-Dar Qutni: 2 - Book of Menstruation, no. 845). Principle: When the blood of the third menses stops at ten days, the time for revocation ends and the iddah is completed.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage deals with the precise moment when the iddah ends after the third menses. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, if the blood of the third menses stops at exactly ten days (the maximum duration of menstruation), the iddah ends immediately even if the woman has not performed ghusl (ritual purification). If the blood stops before ten days, the revocation period does not cease until she performs ghusl, or the time for one prayer passes, or she performs tayammum and prays. This means that the husband may still revoke even after the bleeding stops, as long as she has not purified herself. Imam Muhammad differs: he says that if she performs tayammum, the revocation ceases even if she does not pray. The evidence for the position of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf is the statement of Umar and Abdullah ibn Mas'ud: 'He has more right to her until she performs ghusl from the third menses.' This indicates that ghusl is the marker for the end of the revocation period. Imam Muhammad's evidence is the hadith of Abu Umamah which sets the maximum duration of menstruation at ten days. From this, Muhammad deduces that when the blood stops, the woman enters a state of purity, and if she performs tayammum (which is a substitute for ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful), that purity is established, and the revocation period ends. The Urdu note summarizes: when the blood of the third menses stops at ten days, the revocation time ends and the iddah is completed.

Key Terms
الْخَيْضَةُ الثَّالِثَةُ (al-khaydah al-thalithah): The third menstrual period after divorce, which completes the iddah for a woman who menstruates.
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with clean earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The right of a husband to revoke a revocable divorce during the wife's iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the blood from the third menses stops at seven days, and the woman immediately performs tayammum but does not pray, who among the Hanafi imams says the revocation period ends?
A) Abu Hanifa
B) Abu Yusuf
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The revocation period after the third menses ends only upon full purification (ghusl or tayammum with prayer) according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Disputes on Revocation After Iddah: Hanafi Rulings
Ruling on post-iddah revocation disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on disputes regarding revocation (raj‘ah) after the iddah ends in three distinct scenarios and apply the rule that the wife’s word is accepted without oath.

Core Idea
After iddah ends, the wife's word on revocation is accepted without oath.

This passage from Al-Quduri presents three scenarios where a husband claims he revoked a revocable divorce during the iddah, but the claim is made after the iddah has ended. The rulings hinge on who confirms or denies the claim, and they establish a key principle: in matters known only to the wife, her testimony prevails, and no oath is required from her according to Abu Hanifa.

Guided Path
1
Scenario 1: Wife confirms
If the husband claims he revoked during the iddah and the wife confirms his claim, the revocation is valid.
Confirmation by the wife establishes the raj‘ah as a fact.
2
Scenario 2: Wife denies
If the wife denies the husband's claim, her word is accepted without any oath, according to Abu Hanifa.
The basis is that the wife has direct knowledge of whether a revocation occurred; she is not required to swear an oath.
3
Scenario 3: Wife claims iddah already ended
If the husband says 'I have revoked you' and the wife replies 'My iddah has ended,' the revocation is not valid because the wife’s statement about her own iddah is credible.
This applies even if she is ‘pleasing to him’ – her word about the end of her iddah is decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا انْقَضَتْ الْعِدَّةُ فَقَالَ قَدْ كُنْت رَاجَعْتهَا فِي الْعِدَّةِ فَصَدَّقَتْهُ فَهِيَ رَجْعَةٌ وَإِنْ كَذَّبَتْهُ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلهَا وَلَا يَمِينَ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ ه وَإِذَا قَالَ الزَّوْجُ قَدْ رَاجَعْتُكَ فَقَالَتْ مُحِيبَةً لَهُ قَدْ انْقَضَتْ عِدَّتِي لَمْ تَصِحَ الرَّجْعَةُ عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَإِذَا قَالَ زَوْجُ الْأَمَةِ بَعْدَ انْقِضَاءِ عِدَّتِهَا قَدْ كُنْت رَاجَعْتهَا فَصَدَّقَهُ الْمَوْلَى وَكَذَّبَتْهُ الْأَمَةُ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلها
When the waiting period (iddah) ends, and the husband says, 'I had revoked (raj'ah) her during the iddah,' and she confirms him, then it is a valid revocation. If she denies him, then her word is accepted, and there is no oath according to Abu Hanifa. When the husband says, 'I have revoked you,' and she, being pleasing to him, says, 'My iddah has ended,' the revocation is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. When the husband of a slave woman says after the end of her iddah, 'I had revoked her,' and the master confirms him while the slave woman denies him, then the word is that of the master (or her word? The text says 'qawluhā' but appears ambiguous; the context suggests the slave woman's word is accepted? Actually the text says 'فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلها' which likely means her word is taken, but there is an extra 'لا'? The OCR has 'قَوْلها' without alif. The passage means: her word (the slave woman's) is accepted. Commentary will clarify.)
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from Al-Quduri on disputes concerning revocation (raj'ah) after the iddah has ended. The passage presents three scenarios: (1) If the husband claims he revoked during iddah and the wife confirms, the revocation is valid. (2) If the wife denies, her word is accepted without oath according to Abu Hanifa—this is based on the principle that the wife knows best about her iddah and the occurrence of revocation. (3) If the husband says 'I revoked you' and the wife responds 'My iddah has ended' (implying no revocation), the revocation is not valid per Abu Hanifa because the wife's statement about her own iddah is credible. For a slave woman, if the master confirms the husband's claim but the slave woman denies, the slave woman's word is accepted (according to one interpretation; the text 'قَوْلها' indicates her word prevails). These rulings emphasize that in matters of iddah and revocation, the wife's testimony is given priority due to her direct knowledge.

Key Terms
الرَّجْعَة (al-raj‘ah): Revocation of a revocable divorce during the waiting period, allowing the husband to take back his wife without a new marriage contract.
الْعِدَّة (al-iddah): The waiting period a divorced woman must observe before remarrying, to confirm she is not pregnant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The husband's word is taken with an oath
B) The wife's word is taken without an oath
C) The husband must produce witnesses
D) The revocation is automatically valid

Key Takeaway
After the iddah ends, the wife's word on the occurrence of revocation (raj‘ah) is accepted without oath, protecting her from false claims.

End of Iddah and the Right of Revocation
Ruling on the termination of iddah and its effect on revocation rights.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the iddah ends according to the Hanafi school and explain the conditions that terminate the right of revocation.

Core Idea
The right to revoke a divorce ends only when the woman becomes ritually pure after the third menstruation.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a crucial moment in family law: the exact point at which a revocable divorce becomes final. The ruling hinges on the completion of the third menstrual cycle and the woman's state of purity. We will dissect the conditions and the differing views within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Separate the case where blood stops at exactly ten days from the case where it stops before ten days. The ruling differs for each.
2
Analyze the first scenario (ten days)
If blood stops at the tenth day, the iddah ends immediately even if she has not performed ghusl. Note that no purification is required.
3
Analyze the second scenario (less than ten days)
If blood stops before ten days, the iddah does not end until she performs ghusl, or if water is unavailable, until she performs tayammum and prays (according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
Muhammad holds that tayammum alone suffices without prayer.
4
Understand the ikhtilaf
Compare the positions: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf require prayer after tayammum; Muhammad does not. The mufta bihi follows the majority view.
The difference affects when the husband loses his right to revoke.
5
Apply the principle
The underlying principle is that the woman must be in a state of ritual purity (tubr) for the iddah to end. Ghusl or tayammum with prayer achieves this.
Tayammum alone is considered insufficient by the majority because prayer is the act that demonstrates purity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا انْقَطَعَ الدَّمُ مِنْ الْخَيْضَةِ الثَّالِثَةِ لِعَشْرَةِ أَيَّامٍ انْقَضَتْ الْعِدَّةُ وَإِنْ لَمْ تَغْتَسِلُ وَإِنْ انْقَطَعَ لِأَقَلَّ مِنْ عَشْرَةِ أَيَّامٍ لَمْ تَنْقَطِعْ الرَّجْعَةُ حَتَّى تَغْتَسِلَ أَوْ يَمْضِيَ عَلَيْهَا وَقْتُ الصَّلَاةِ أَوْ تَتَيَمَّمُ وَتُصَلِّي عِنْدَ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ وَأَبِي يُوسُفَ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ إِذَا تَيَمَّمَتْ انْقَطَعَتْ الرَّجْعَةُ وَإِنْ لَمْ تُصَلّ
When blood ceases from the third menstruation after ten days, the iddah ends even if she has not performed ghusl. If it ceases before ten days, the revocation is not cut off until she performs ghusl, or the time of prayer passes over her, or she performs tayammum and prays according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: If she performs tayammum, the revocation is cut off even if she does not pray.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri deals with the precise moment when a revocable divorce becomes irrevocable—when the iddah ends. For a woman whose iddah is by menstrual cycles, the third menses marks the end. If the third menses lasts a full ten days (the maximum), upon cessation of blood, the iddah ends immediately regardless of ghusl. If the bleeding stops before ten days, the iddah does not end until she performs ghusl (full bath) to purify herself, or if she is unable, until she performs tayammum and prays. The ruling differs among the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf require either ghusl, the passing of a prayer time, or tayammum with prayer; while Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that tayammum alone cuts off the right of revocation even without prayer. The underlying principle is that the woman becomes ritually pure and thus the iddah terminates, allowing her to remarry. The proofs cited include a hadith about the maximum menstruation being ten days, and a narration from Umar and Abdullah that the husband has the right to revocation until she performs ghusl from the third menses.

Key Terms
الْخَيْضَة (al-khaydah): Menstruation; a period of menstrual bleeding.
التَّيَمُّم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution with earth or dust as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful.
الرَّجْعَة (al-raj‘ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during the iddah without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately upon cessation of blood
B) Only after she performs ghusl or prays after tayammum
C) After ten days regardless of ghusl
D) Never, as revocation is always possible

Key Takeaway
The end of iddah and the right of revocation depend on the woman's purification; the Hanafi school requires ghusl or, if water is unavailable, tayammum followed by prayer to terminate the right of revocation.

Incomplete Ghusl and Its Effect on Revocation
Ruling on incomplete ghusl and revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on incomplete ghusl during iddah and apply the 'complete limb' criterion to determine whether the right of revocation remains.

Core Idea
Missing a full limb invalidates ghusl; less than a limb does not—and revocation depends on that.

This lesson examines a specific scenario at the intersection of taharah and talaq: a woman in her iddah performs ghusl after the third menses but forgets to wash a part of her body. The ruling hinges on the size of the missed area—whether it constitutes a complete limb or something smaller. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly determining when the iddah ends and the husband's right to revoke is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is ghusl performed by a woman whose iddah has ended with the third menses. This ghusl is intended to return her to a state of ritual purity, which is a condition for the iddah to be complete.
The context is iddah after a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).
2
Recognize the two outcomes based on the missed area
The ruling separates two cases: if the missed area is a complete limb (udw kamil) or more, the ghusl is incomplete and revocation continues. If it is less than a limb, the ghusl is considered valid and revocation is cut off.
A 'limb' refers to a major body part like an arm, leg, or head.
3
Apply the criterion to a new scenario
Take a woman who forgets to wash her entire left arm. Since the arm is a complete limb, the ghusl is invalid, so the husband can still revoke. If she forgets only her elbow (less than a whole arm), the ghusl is valid and revocation ends.
The ruling is binary: limb-or-more = no purity; less-than-limb = purity achieved.
4
Understand the underlying principle
The Hanafi view is that a minor omission does not invalidate ghusl, but a major omission (whole limb) does. This is based on the idea that a missed limb represents a substantial failure to wash the body.
Other schools may define 'limb' differently or have different thresholds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَإِنْ اغْتَسَلَتْ وَنَسِيَتْ شَيْئًا مِنْ بَدَنِهَا لَمْ يُصِبْهُ الْمَاءُ فَإِنْ كَانَ عُضْوًا كَامِلًا فَمَا فَوْقَهُ لَمْ تَنْقَطِعْ الرَّجْعَةُ وَإِنْ كَانَ أَقَلَّ مِنْ عُضْوِ انْقَطَعَتْ
So if she performed ghusl and forgot something from her body that water did not reach, then if it was a complete limb or more, the revocation is not cut off; and if it was less than a limb, it is cut off.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This ruling addresses a scenario where a woman whose iddah has ended with the third menses performs ghusl but inadvertently misses washing some part of her body. The rule distinguishes based on the size of the missed area: if she missed an entire limb (like an arm or leg) or more, then the ghusl is considered incomplete, and she is still in a state of ritual impurity; consequently, the husband's right to revoke the divorce remains, as she has not fully purified. If she missed only a small area less than a full limb, the ghusl is considered valid enough to remove impurity, and thus the iddah ends, cutting off the right of revocation. This is based on the principle that a minor omission does not invalidate the ghusl, but a major omission does. The Hanafi school defines a 'limb' (udw) as one of the main body parts: head, neck, arms, legs, etc. The underlying wisdom is that the iddah ending is tied to the woman becoming ritually pure, and that purity is achieved only when ghusl is properly complete.

Key Terms
عُضْو (udw): A limb or major body part; for ghusl, washing the whole body is required, and missing a complete limb invalidates it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Revocation ends immediately
B) Revocation remains because the ghusl is incomplete
C) Revocation remains only if she forgot more than one limb
D) It depends on her intention

Key Takeaway
The validity of ghusl for ending iddah depends on whether the missed area is a complete limb or more; if so, revocation continues.

Etiquette During Revocable Divorce
Ruling on recommended behavior for revocably divorced woman and husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the recommended behaviors for a revocably divorced woman and her husband during the iddah period according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A revocably divorced woman should adorn herself for her husband, and he should announce his presence.

The period of revocable divorce (talaq raj'i) is not a complete severance; the marriage bond remains intact until the iddah expires. In this passage, Imam al-Quduri outlines the recommended etiquette for both spouses to encourage reconciliation and maintain proper conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal status of the woman
The woman is described as 'al-mutallaqatu ar-raj'iyyah' – revocably divorced. This status implies that the marriage is not fully dissolved; she remains his wife in some legal aspects.
2
Classify the ruling level of the acts
The text uses the verb 'yustahabb' (it is recommended). In Hanafi fiqh, this indicates a sunnah muakkadah (confirmed recommended act), not an obligation.
3
Specify the woman's recommended act
She is to 'tatashawwaq' (long for) and 'tatazayyan' (adorn herself). These acts express her willingness to reconcile with her husband.
Tatashawwaq may also be read as tatashawwaf in some texts.
4
Specify the husband's recommended act
He should not enter upon her until he informs her or makes his presence known, e.g., by the sound of his sandals. This shows respect and avoids startling her.
5
Recognize the legal effect
These acts are not obligatory but are strongly encouraged to facilitate reconciliation. Failure to do them is not sinful but is contrary to optimal conduct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُطَلَّقَةُ الرَّجْعِيَّةُ تَتَشَوَّقُ وَتَتَزَيَّنُ وَيُسْتَحَبُّ لِزَوْجِهَا أَنْ لَا يَدْخُلَ عَلَيْهَا حَتَّى يُؤْذِنَهَا أَوْ يُسْمِعَهَا خَفْقَ نَعْلَيْهِ
The revocably divorced woman should long for (her husband) and adorn herself. It is recommended for her husband that he should not enter upon her until he informs her or makes her hear the sound of his sandals (i.e., announces his presence).
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage provides guidance on the conduct during the period of revocable divorce. The woman is encouraged to beautify herself and show longing for her husband, as the marriage is still intact and there is hope for reconciliation. This is recommended (mustahabb) to facilitate a positive reunion. The husband, on the other hand, is advised to announce his presence before entering upon her, to avoid startling her and to maintain modesty. This is based on the understanding that the woman is still his wife in many respects, but they are living apart during the iddah. The proofs cited include a narration from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i that she may apply kohl and wear colored garments. The husband's practice of announcing his arrival is supported by the action of Abdullah ibn Umar, who used to seek permission when visiting his divorced wife.

Key Terms
تَتَشَوَّق (tatashawwaq): To long for or show affection towards her husband; to behave in a loving manner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
A revocably divorced woman is encouraged to adorn herself and show affection for her husband, and the husband should announce his presence, all to facilitate reconciliation.

Types of Divorce and Their Effects on Remarriage
Rulings on revocable, irrevocable, and triple divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three categories of divorce (raj‘i, ba‘in without completion, and triple) and state the rulings on intercourse and remarriage for each.

Core Idea
A revocable divorce permits intercourse; an irrevocable (less than three) permits remarriage; triple divorce requires a complete intervening marriage.

Divorce in Hanafi law is not a single act but a spectrum with distinct legal consequences. This passage from Al-Quduri presents the three core types — revocable, irrevocable (less than three), and triple — and specifies what is permissible and prohibited in each case. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly advising on post-divorce relationships and the possibility of remarriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first category: revocable divorce
Find the phrase 'al-talaq al-raj‘i' and note its ruling: it does not prohibit sexual intercourse during the iddah. Intercourse itself counts as revocation.
Revocable divorce applies to the first or second pronouncement.
2
Identify the second category: irrevocable divorce less than three
The passage states that if the divorce is ba‘in (irrevocable) but less than the third, the husband may marry her again with a new contract during or after her iddah.
This is ba‘in baynuna sughra; the marriage is dissolved but remarriage is possible without an intervening husband.
3
Identify the third category: triple divorce (free woman) or double (slave woman)
When divorce is pronounced three times for a free woman or twice for a slave woman, she becomes completely prohibited to him until she marries another man in a valid marriage, consummates it, and then is divorced or widowed.
This is ba‘in baynuna kubra; the woman must undergo tahlil.
4
Note the key difference between ba‘in and triple
In ba‘in less than three, remarriage is possible immediately with a new contract. In triple, remarriage is impossible unless she marries another and is separated.
The condition for halal after triple is rigorous: valid nikah, dukhul, and separation.
5
State the condition for halal after triple divorce
The wife must marry another man in a valid marriage (nikah sahih), consummate with him (dukhul), and then be divorced or widowed. Only then does she become lawful to her first husband.
Temporary marriage (mut‘ah) does not fulfill this condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْل: وَالطَّلَاقُ الرَّجْعِيُّ لَا يُحَرِّمُ الْوَطْءَ وَإِذَا كَانَ الطَّلَاقُ بَائِنَا دُونَ الثَّلَاثِ فَلَهُ أَنْ يَتَزَوَّجَهَا فِي عِدَّتِهَا وَبَعْدَ انْقِضَاءِ عِدَّتِهَا وَإِذَا كَانَ الطَّلَاقُ ثَلَاثًا فِي الْحَرَّةِ أَوْ اثْنَتَيْنِ فِي الْأَمَةِ لَمْ تَحِلَّ لَهُ حَتَّى تَنْكِحَ زَوْجًا غَيْرَهُ نِكَاحًا صَحِيحًا وَيَدْخُلَ بِهَا ثُمَّ يُطَلِّقَهَا أَوْ يَمُوتَ عَنْهَا
Section: Revocable divorce does not prohibit intercourse. When the divorce is irrevocable but less than three, then he may marry her during her iddah and after its completion. When the divorce is three times for a free woman or twice for a slave woman, she is not lawful for him until she marries another husband in a valid marriage and he consummates with her, then he divorces her or dies from her.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This concise passage summarizes the core legal distinctions between types of divorce in Hanafi fiqh. (1) Revocable divorce (talaq raj'i): the husband can have sexual intercourse with his wife during iddah, and that intercourse itself constitutes a revocation. (2) Irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) less than three: the marriage is dissolved, but he may remarry her with a new marriage contract during or after iddah. (3) Triple divorce (talaq thalath) for a free woman, or double divorce for a slave woman: the woman becomes completely prohibited to the husband until she marries another man, consummates that marriage, and then is divorced or widowed (this is the halal principle, known as tahlil). These rulings are derived from the Quranic verses: "Divorce is twice, then retention in kindness or release with goodness" (2:229) for the first two divorces, and "If he divorces her [a third time], she is not lawful to him until she marries another husband" (2:230). The proofs cited include the Quranic verses and statements from early authorities. The school's position is that the first two divorces are revocable and the third makes it irrevocable unless she marries another.

Key Terms
الطَّلَاقُ الرَّجْعِيُّ (al-talaq al-raj'i): Revocable divorce, where the husband can take back his wife without a new contract during the iddah.
الطَّلَاقُ الْبَائِن (al-talaq al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce, where the marriage is dissolved and cannot be renewed except by a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, after a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i), is sexual intercourse between the spouses permissible?
A) No, it is completely prohibited
B) Yes, it is permissible and counts as revocation
C) Only if she agrees
D) Only after the iddah ends

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of intercourse and remarriage after divorce depends critically on the type of divorce: revocable permits intercourse, ba‘in less than three allows remarriage, and triple requires a complete intervening marriage.
